These were the only

The emissaries added that if
peace was forced upon them it
would not be permanent.'*
The Filipino refugees are all
gathered at San Fernando. The
insurgent soldiers are looting
and are said to be in a state of
mutiny. Peasants in large num
bers are returning to their homes
within our territory.
New telegraphic connections
with General Lawton by way of
Bocave were completed to-night.
The insurgents attacked the
Americans at Naguig yesterday,
killing two of our men and
wounding twelve. They were I
subsequently repulsed by five

companies of the First Washing

have one piece of artillery mount

noon and asked for American
protection. They want ( ieneval
Mac Arthur to occupy San Fer
nando, where they report that
the insurgents are looting and
destroying property.
FILIPINOS' ADVANCES
HAVE BEEN FRUITLESS
MANILA, April 28.-The Filipino ad
vances for peace at terms which would
b>- in any way advantageous to Aguin
aldo have been fruitless. Colonel Man
uel Arguelezes and Lieutenant Jose
Rernal, who came into General Mac-
Arthurp lines under h flag of truce, told
General Otis that they were represen
tatives '>f General Luna, who had been
requested by Agulnaldo to ask for a
cessation of hostilities in order to al
low time for the summoning of the Fil
ipino congress, which body would <i>-
cide whether the people wanted peace.
General otis rr-pli^d he did
no* recognize the existence <>f the Fili
pino government. Thf-rÂ«' will b"
another conference, to-morrow.
The Filipino officers walked down the
railroad track to the Kansas regiment's
outpost at 9 o'clock this morning. The
Kansas captain in command there es
corted them to General AVhfatnn's
headquarters, where they were pro
vided with horses and s.-nt to the head
quarters of General HacArthur. The
hitter invited the Filipinos to sit down
at lunch with him and conversed with
them for some time. lie refused, how
ever, to speak authoritatively on the
subject of their errand, referring all in
quiries to General Otis. The Filipinos
were then escorted by Major Malloney
of General Mao Arthur's staff to Ma
nila, reaching this place at 3 p. m.
General Otis' aid. Lieutenant Sladen,
was awaiting their arrival at the d< poi
with a carriage, in which they were
driven to the palace entrance. The
Filipino officers attracted much atten
tion. They were dressed in uniforms
of checked blue and white cloth and
wore straw hats. They carried no side
arms. They were escorted directly to
the office of General Otis. Jacob B.
Schurman, president of the Philippine
Commission, and Hon. Charles Denby,
a member of the commission, soon
joined the party there. The news of
the arrival of Filipino officers under a
flag of truce spread through the city
rapidly and many officers gravitated
SAN FRANCISCO, SATURDAY, APRIL 29, 1899.
[ to the corridors of the palace, while a
: crowd of native's gathered in the square
; opposite.
At f> o'clock the two Filipino officers.
escorted by Lieutenant Sladen and
Major Malloney, left the palace. They
did not look at all elated as a result
of their meeting with C.-eneral Otis and
the members of the Filipino Commis
sion.
In offering to submit a peace propo
sition to his so-called Congress Agui
naldo hopes by subterfuge to overcome
General otis' consistent policy of ig
noring the Filipino Government. The
Filipinos' argument is that it is im
possible to arrange an armistice with
out tho sanction of the Congress.
General <Â»tis punctured this assump
tion to-day by asking, the emissaries
if General Aguinaldo could make war
without the sanction of Congress, why
he could not stop without reference to
that body?
Whil<- the insurgents are undoubted
ly tired of war, the loaders are torn
with dissensions. There is ;i suspicion
that it was hoped by means of a con
ferehce to ascertain what they could
expect. If they saw that anything was
to he gained by continuing the war, an
armistice would afford them an oppor
tunity for recuperating their demoral
ized forces.
It is an- interesting commentary upon
Aguinaldo's proposition that only sixty
of the 300 members of the Filipino <'on
gress have taken the oath of allegi
ance which their constitution requires.
A Filipino proclamation replying to
the proclamation of the American
Commissioners has appeared. It is
signed by Mabini for the President and
is dated at Caniasdro, April 15. It is
in the usual grandiose style, and de
clares that President McKinley Issued
the proclamation in order to force the
American Congress to ratify the ces
sion of the islands under the treaty of
Paris.
"This contract Of cession was made
with the Spanish after Spanish domina
tion had been ended by the valor of our
troops," the writer asserts. The procla
mation complains that the Filipinos
were not represented at Paris during
the negotiation of the treaty and that
they are without assurances of the ful
fillment of American promises. It di
lates upon the alleged Anglo-Saxon
hatred of blacks, and asserts a desire
to enslave them. Deploring a lack of
foreign aid in prosecuting the war the
proclamation concludes:
"We stand alone, but we will fight j
to the death. Coming generations will
pray over our graves, shedding tears
of gratitude for their freedom."
Yesterday furnished addei proof that
the T T Tiited States troops have a fear
of bushwhacking that is above their
fears cf battle. The Washington Regi
ment, which is holding Taguig with
three companies of the Twelfth regu
lars, engaged a large force of insur
gents in a fight in the jungle. The
Americans lost two killed and ten
wounded.
The Filipinos have been massing at
the lake mouth of the Pasig River, and
it is estimated that there are 2000 of
them now there. They have mounted
two gunsâ one a three-inch Krupp â
and have thrown' a number of shells
into the American lines. The gunboat
Rapldan, which is guarding the en
trance to the river, shelled a launch
which was carrying supplies.
Yesterday a large force of rebels ap
proached the town, seemingly bent on
luring the Americans from <'alumpit.
Three companies of the Washington
troops sallied from the town and
attacked the natives and found that
they had their hands full. Two other
companies of the Washingtons and
three of the Twelfth regulars rein
forced them and drove back the
enemy, who removed their dead and
Wounded as they retired, as usual.

position of the administration with re
spect to the Filipinos:
"Negotiations for peace will be con
ducted by the Philippine Commission
and not by General Otis alone. Before
negotiations can be begun the Filipinos
will be required to lay down their arms.
"When they have given this evidence of
their intention to abandon them the
War Commission will be authorized to
announce to them the intentions of the
Government.
"There is no disposition on the part
of the President to punish Aguinaldo
or any other Filipino. The insurgent
leader will be treated like any other
native. Should he desire a special

When American supremacy is estab
lished in the island of Luzon the au
thorities think there will be a prompt
collapse of the revolt in the island of
Panay. Natives of other Philippine
islands have been watching with k"i n
interest the course of affairs in Luzon,
and as they have been stirred up by
Aguinaldo's emissaries the defeat' of
Aguinaldo and his army will un
doubtedly have a good effect through
out the archipelago.
After concluding peace with Agui
naldo's army the commission will ar
range for visiting other islands to con
fer with the natives concerning re
forms they desire and to determine the
character of legislation for the. govern
ment of the islands which should te
recommended to the President for pre
sentation to Congress.
The terms which will be offered to the
Filipinos will not differ materially from
those stated in the recent proclamation
issued by the commission. The comm
ission then declared the Philippine
people would be granted the largest lib
erty and self-government reconcilable
with the maintenance of a wise, Just,
effective and economical administration
of public affairs and compatible with
the sovereign rights and obligations of
the United States. Civil rights of the
Filipinos will be granted to the fullest
extent. Religious freedom will be as
sured and all persons will have equal
standing before the law.
While in sonic quarters there is a
disposition to think that th- 1 Filipinos
have made this move simply to gain
time, high officials think the action of
Aguinaldo is due to the fact that he
and his troops have had enough ol
fighting American forces.
As General Otis has undoubtedly re
fused the armistice requested, it is ap
parent that Aguinaldo has really lost

VOLUNTEERS AS ALLIES
OF THE ADMINISTRATION
NEW YORK, April 28.â A Washing
ton special t" the Herald says: Presi
dent McKinley and the administration
have been relieved of a great load of
anxiety by the appan nt collapse of the
Philippine Insurrection. They have
hoped for an early termination of rh"
foniiict with Aeruinaldn not only be
cause it would stop the shedding or
blood and would relieve them of the
necessity for calling for more volun
teers, but because it meant much to the
administration and the party politi
cally.
The restoration of peare would en
able the President to turn the Philip
pine problem over to Congress in De
cember in much better shape than
would have been possible had the war
still been soing- on. By that time he
will have put into operation under
military authority a form of govern
ment which the Philippine Commission
will have "recommended. He will have
the report of that commission and be
able to give Congress valuable infor
mation as to tin' situation in the
islands and the capabilities of the peo
ple for self-government.
There is no doubt that this commis
sion will report in favor of the United
States retaining: the islands indefi
nitely, and such a policy will be much2
better received by tho country with
peace restored and trade beginning to
develop than would be the case with a
war in progress and more soldiers be
ing sent to the front.
Returned volunteers arc expected to
be strong allies of the administration in
the policy of retaining the islands. They
Will be treated with the greatest con
sideration, and every one of them will
be proud Of the part he has taken in
wresting the islands from the Spanish
and subduing the rebellious natives.
They will not. it is believed, favor any
proposition to abandon the fruits of
their valor.

OTIS DENIES STORIES
OF AMERICAN ATROCITIES
NEW YORK, April 28.â A Washington
special to the Herald says: General Otis
denies the reports of American atrocities
in the Philippines as described by Charles
Brenner in a recent letter to his home.
In answer to a cablegram of inquiry he
haÃ informed the Secretary of War that
none of the incidents referred to by
Brenner have ever been brought to his
attention, and that he has been unable
thus far to find any foundation for the re
ports. He states, however, that he will
continue the Investigation.

CONTRACT LABOR
SYSTEM RESTORED
Hawaii Is Open to the
Coolies.
HONOLULU. April 22.â 0n the 17th
President Dole and his Cabinet decided
i not to allow any additional contract
| laborers to come into the country. This
] decision was arrived at after several
I conferences had been held with the
! planters. In the meantime word has
! been received from Washington which
j restores the old order of things and
j now the planters may import as many
| laborers as they may require.
By the Nippon Maru President Dole
received a note from the Treasury De
partment stating that the annexation
i resolution did not and does not effect
j the labor system of this country, and
! that the same remains in force and
I effect until such time as Congress may
j enact laws changing it. The present
! applications for contract Japanese for
I the new plantations will be approved
i by the Government and forwarded by
I the next steamer.
President Dole this morning admitted
the receipt of an official decision from
i Washington in favor of the old labor
: system.
"Yes," added the Preside/it, "the let
i ter reopens the matter. We will have
i a meeting as soon as possible with the
planters and inform them of the
i changtd situation."
Asked if the situation was not now
| identical with that before the Hawaiian
Cabinet had formed its recent opinion,
! the President replied:
"Yes; probably. That is the point
upon which we wish to see the planters.
, It may still seem advisable to impose
\ some restrictions."
The Government to-day decided to
) address a letter to the planters asking
1 them to file applications for such labor
as they require.
Between 300 and 400 Japanese immi-
I grants arrived on the America Maru
i to-day. They come to enter into labor
j contracts under* permission given by
the Government.
It is understood that the policy of
] the planters with respect to the future
1 will be about as follows: A gradual
reduction of the use of contract Asiatic
| labor and a corresponding Introduction
! of free American help Into the cane
! fields. The delay in the application of
I American laws will enable the planters
to adopt American labor rules by de
grees, and will insure the country
against the damaging effects of a sud
den change. Colonel Whyte's project
for bringing in American labor will re
ceive early attention. There probably
will be a company, known as the Amer
'â¢ lean Labor Supply Company, or some
i thing very similar, backed by the plan
i tation agencies. It is hoped that with
i in a few months this concern will have
j its labor channels thoroughly opened,
| and that by December it will be in a
position to supply all the workmen re-
I quired.

GAGE LOOKS OUT
FOR HIS LAMBS
Rewards for Supporters
of Burns.
Special Dispatch, to The Call. -
LOS ANGELES, April That Colonel
Dan M. Burns is in absolute control of the
'patronage of the Republican party, of Cal
ifornia and that those who were elected
to office last November must take coun
sel with him was made evident to-day to
all office-seekers when George Washing
ton Root of San Francisco, clerk of the
Supreme Court, announced that Harold
G. Simpson of Pasadena had been chosen
as the Los Angeles County deputy clerk
of the court. The appointee is .the son
of Senator C. M. Simpson of Pasadena.
The contest for this uillet has been very
keen as it pays $150 a month for four
years The leading candidates, aside from
Simpson, were John Hughes and Frank
Dominguez, both prominent in the local
"push, and who were. In the front In the
Gage ranks. . Â
When Simpson boasted, on the day he
disregarded his county delegation's In
! structions and voted for Burns, that he
â¢ had been cared for his friends thought the
statement was a burst of humor, but now
he is enjoying loaves and fishes. As Sen
ator Simpson at the time of casting his
vote for Burns was entering upon his new
four-year term, he has nothing further to
ask from his constituency, as his -own
term and that of his son will expire about
the same time.
Harold G. Simpson, Â«he appointee, is
probably the youngest man who ever held
a similar position anywhere. He is only
23 years old, but is said to be exceptional
ly capable. Young Simpson was chief bill
filer in the Senate during the recent legis
lative session. He will take charge of the
office on May 1.
W. M. Ward, who has been in the office
temporarily, will leave In about two
weeks for San Francisco, which is his
permanent station.

REGARDING SAMOA
BERLIN, April 28.â The newspapers
here, commenting to-day on the latest
news from Samoa, express satisfaction
with the attitude of Admiral Kautz, U.
S. N., interpreting It aa a sign that the
United States Government will hence
forth side with Germany In the Samoan
troubles. The Boersen Courier says that
the American admiral's position may indi
cate how differently the Americans and
the English in Samoa Interpret Instruc
tions received simultaneously.
The Neusten Nachrichten notes "with
lively satisfaction" the loyal attitude of
the Washington Government "In admon
ishing Admiral Kautz in relation to his
letter to his cousin in the United States."
It is believed here that an understand
ing has been reached between the Gov
ernments of the United States and Ger
many not only as to their relations in
Samoa but In regard to other portions of
the world as well. Some of the Berlin
newspapers to-day condemn a speech
made recently by the United States Con
sul at Amsterdam, George C. Correy, in
which it was said that from the year 1868
Prince Bismarck had been constantly
scheming to seize Holland. The North
German Gazette, commenting on this as
sertion, says that the United States seems
to pick out singular specimens of man
kind for diplomatic posts.

SURGEON'S MISTAKE
BLINDS A PATIENT
Remove a Sound Eye prom o
Boy Instead of One That
Was Sightless.
MONTREAL, April 28.â Seven years ago, Thomas Stewart, then 10
years old, lost the sight of one eye, the blade of a penknife having
been accidentally run into it. Dr. Alexander Froudfoot attended him.
Recently Dr. Proudfoot advised the removal of the useless eye as the
only means of preserving the other intact. This was agreed to and the
delicate task was entrusted to Dr. Proudfoot, who is assistant oculist
and aurist to the general hospital.
The operation took place at the family residence and at its conclu
sion it was found a terrible mistake had been madeâ the healthy eye had
been removed. On recovering from the effects of the anesthetic the
patient found himself blind.
ENTOMBED ALIVE
BY CAVING EARTH
Horrible Fate of Three
Well-Diggers.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
CLARKS STATION, New, April 28.â
Three of a gang of workmen which had
been repairing a well 100 yards from the
j station here were buried at 8 o'clock
this morning beneath fifteen or twenty
feet of earth and timbers by the giving
way of the curbing. The soil is very
Bandy, and as some braces were re
moved for the purpose of putting in a
new curbing, the entire structure at the
top gave way, the men falling to the
bottom, with caved-in earth and broken
timbers on top of them. Immediately
i after the accident a rapping on the iron
! pip^ which extends from the surface to
\ the oottom could be heard, showing
that at least one of the unfortunates
was still alive. A special train with
forty laborers^ and two physicians was
hurried from Reno, but before it ar
rived the rapping had ceased.
The work of digging out the well was
hurried throughout the day, and at 6
6'clock this evening the rescuers were
within four feet of the imprisoned men.
As water was struck during the after
noon, and as the steam pump which
was put into operation this afternoon
cannot keep puce with the fast-rising
water, there is no possibility that any
of the entombed men are now alive.
This evening quicksand was encoun
tered and digging operations ceased
while new curbing and walling were
being constructed. At a lute hour to
night the force had resumed digging
! and will keep steadily at it until th"
bodies are reached.
The victims* are John Downey, a
Scotchman, aged about 30. who had a
brother in Minneapolis, Minn., and a
sister in Scotland; Robert A. Fairburn
(Or Fairbairn). who had a wife and
family in Oak Park. Sacramento; Man
uel Francisco, single, an Italian, whose
only known relative was a cousin in
Reno. _____________

STANFORD, April 28.â 0n Monday,
Dewey day, Stanford will cross bats with
Santa Clara at San Jose. The pr<
of the game will be given to charity.HUNTINGTON AND SCHWERIN EMPLOY WASHINGTON LOBBYISTS TO PROTECT
THE DISHONEST CHINESE PASSENGER TRAFFIC OF THE PACIFIC MAIL
THspatches received from Washington
last night throw a new and startling
light upon the secret causes of the dis
missal of R. E. Meredith as chief of
the Chinese Bureau of this city. Mere
dith was discharged from his posi
tion of trust for cause. The Call, in its
endeavor to remedy the glaring abuses
of the Chinese Bureau, exposed the
methods of Meredith, and to-day it will
expose the powerful corporations which
stood sponsor for Meredith in his ma
nipulations. The Chinese Bureau of
this city is the only practical barrier
on the Pacific Coast to the uninter
rupted influx of Chinese coolies to the
United States.
The officers of the bureau are ex
pected to be honest, efficient and vigi
lant. In their hands rests the vitally
important duty of enforcing the exclu
sion act and of preventing the degrad
ed laborers of the Orient from enter
ing the port of San Francisco. Mere- j
dith, the chief of the bureau, was dis- I
missed from the service of the Federal '
Government because he was found j
grossly negligent of his sworn duties.
The Call will show to-day that the j
Southern Pacific Company, C. P. Hunt- !
ington, the Pacific Mail Steamship \
Company and R. P. Schwerin were the i
pupporters and sponsors of Meredith, j
his close friends while in office and his j
persist. nt defenders while in disgrace.
The officers of the Southern Pacific
Company and of the Pacific Mail
Steamship Company exercised every j
influence within their power to restore j
Meredith to his position after iv had
been discharged.
They appealed to John P. Jackson, j
Collector of th Port, and secured his
unqualified indorsement of Meredith, in
character and ability. An effort was
made at Washington to induce the Sec
retary of the Treasury to reconsider
his decision of dismissal. Paid lobby
ists of the Southern Pacific Company
and of the Pacific Mail Steamship Com
pany were ordered to use their seduc
tive .'ins upon Federal officials at
Washington and if possible save Mere
dith from dismissal. An .appeal was
even made to President McKinley him- :
self to save from final discharge this
officer whose duty it was to keep Chi
nese out and not to let them in.
The cause of this campaign to save
.-. petty official from dismissal, the mo
tives of telegrams', letters and vehement
personal instruction. might, by a
stretch of the imagination, b,e a mys
tery. But R. P. Schwerin, the protege
and beneficiary of the bounty of C. P.
Huntingtoh"; gives the reason. The
Pacific Mail Steamship Company profits
by the illegitimate traffic in Chinese
coolies. It reaps an ill-gotten harvest
in freights and fares from coulies that
by legislative enactment are barred
from an entrance into the United
States.
Every Chinese who is brought from
China to this port and is then denied a
landing becomes a burden upon the
Pacific Mail. Every Chinese who comes
and. rightly or wrongly, is permitted
to enter is an element of profit to the
corrupt corporation that lives to make
money at whatever cost to the people
it preys upon. The Pacific Mail Steam
ship Company has been and is in
volved in an illegitimate Chinese
traffic. Any measure which Interferes
with that traffic is. to tile officers of
the Pacific Mail, an affront and a tres
pass upon their vicious rights. And
when B. E. Meredith was removed from
his position as chief of the Chinese
Bureau R. P. Schwerin wrote to C. P.
Huntington in New York:
"You must have Meredith rein
stated. His removal means a body
blow at the Chinese passenger traffic
of the Pacific Mail Company."
And C P. Huntington replied:

Much good will be accomplished by
the new mode of enforcing the exclu
sion act. The head of the bureau will
no longer be the creature of the Collec
tor, and will be therefore more inde- I
pendent in the performance of his du
ties. He will be directly responsible to
the Secretary of the Treasury for wh it
ever mistakes he may make, and th^re
will be no collectors and ex-collectors
t.p intercede fur him and furnish cer
tificates of character to save him from
the departmental wrath. He must
stand upon his own feet, and his char
acter must speak, not through hear
say certificates, but through his official |
acts. The trouble with Meredith has
been that his certificates of character
don't dovetail with his official mistakes
which, unfortunately for him, if they
were mistakes, were on the wrong side.
They were invariably on the side- of the
Pacific Mail Steamship Company and
the slave dealers and against the in
terests of the exclusion act and the
Government. No merchant will keep a
clerk who makes mistakes in giving
change against his employer or who
doles out good coins for counterfeit.
Again, if, after Mr. Jackson shall j

should receive the appointment as Col

Wanted to Go to Manila.
James Holmes, a bright boy. 13 years |
of age, was found wandering around the ;
water front early yesterday morning by I
Policeman Bailey. He interrogated the 1
boy and ascertained that he came from i
Buffalo, N. V.. with Company H of the |
Thirteenth Infantry and was going with I
them to Manila, but the transport sailed j
without him. He was hungry, and after
Bailey had fed him he sent him to the J
cent upheaval in the bureau for the
good of the service.
An exposition was held last summer
in Omaha and among the concessions
was one of the Mcc Lee Village Asso
ciation, a Chinese company, which re
produced a Chinese village on the
grounds and gave theatrical and acro
batic entertainments. About five hun
dred men and women of more or lessâ
generally â virtue were imported
from China to take part in the village
show, and they were admitted under a
special privilege granted by the United
States, under the terms of which the
actors, acrobats, shopkeepers and at
tendants were to return to â  China at
the expiration of three months' after
the close of the exposition. It was
found that before the exposition closed
about 200 women, mostly of the boat
men's class, had left Omaha and had
gone to reside in different cities in the
Union, San Francisco getting the most
of them. This becoming known, the
Government ordered Major Moore to or
ganize a raid upon the Chinatown
bagnios in this city for the purpose of
arresting and deporting the # runaways.
Collector Jackson detailed a large force
of customs inspectors to assist in the
raid, but for some reason never made
public the police were informed of the
contemplated movement and were sta
tioned in the houses to be raided, where
they were found a half-hour later by
the Federal posse in full possession
and the whole of Chinatown advised of
what was going on. The Federal posse
were detained at the Custom-house
awaiting the order of Jackson to start.
Notwithstanding the publicity given to
the affair about twenty-seven women
without certificates were captured.
Among the number taken to the Pres
byterian Chinese Mission on Sacra
mento street was a middle-aged woman
named Fong Suey Wan, a notorious
procuress of considerable wealth and
the wife of the partner of Little Pete,
a noted Chinese boss and slave dealer,
lately deceased by the grace of a high
binder's bullet in a Chinatown barber
shop.
When the women were being ques
tioned at the mission Miss Cameron,
the matron, recognized the procuress
and informed Meredith of her identity.
Meredith ordered her to be detained
notwithstanding that her certificate in
due form was then being presented for
inspection. Nothing further was done
by Meredith to hojd the woman. He
filed no complaint against her, he did
not even inform the United States Mar
shal, the District Attorney or any one
else in authority that he proposed to
place any charge against her. On the
next day after her arrest Arthur Lotto,
a reporter, warned Meredith that the
woman would be discharged on pre
sentation of her certificate before Court
Commissioner Heacock if Meredith did
not file a complaint charging her with
being a procuress. Meredith replied
that it was "all right," he was getting
up "a great case" against her. On the
next morning the woman was taken
before Commissioner Heacock, on the
same floor as the Chinese Bureau and
about seventy-five feet distant, and her
certificate being produced, the court
stated that he would recommend her
discharge, there being no complaint ac
cusing her of any offense. She was then
taken to the office of the United States
Marshal, directly underneath the office
of the Chinese Bureau, and kept there
until 2 o'clock in the afternoon, when
she was brought before United States
District Judge de Haven and dis
charged. Then she disappeared.
Meredith could offer no reasonable
explanation as to why he had allowed
the woman to escape, and public
clamor was raised, the episode savor
ing too strongly of corruption to be
passed by in silence. Special Agent
Moore, in pursuance of instructions
from Washington, made an investiga
tion, examining witnesses and taking
affidavits of all parties concerned. He
sent his report to Washington, and
about the time that the department
was looking into the report ex-Collector
Wise appeared in Washington and told
the Treasury people and the newspaper
correspondents a fairy story to the
effect that the woman was allowed to
ge because Major Moore and Miss
Cameron had asked that Fong Suey be
allowed to return to China.
As a matter of justice to Mr. Wise it
must be said that he knew nothing of
the case of his own knowledge, as all
his information had been derived from
Meredith and his friends. Then charges
were filed against Special Agent Moore
for making an assault upon a woman
employed in his office as typewriter,
and the Secretary of the Treasury, in
view of Moore's disgrace, sent Moore's
report back to this city to be verified
by Special Agents Linck and Smith.
The witnesses were re-examined and
the Fong Suey Wan report was sent
on to Washington once more. It was
upon this report, which dealt with the
Fong Suey Wan incident alone, that
Meredith was dismissed.
Subsequently the special agents sent
a supplemental report to Washington
in connection with the matter of Mere
dith having used abusive language to
Dr. Gardiner and a reporter of The
Call, but before it had reached the Sec
retary of the Treasury Meredith had
been dismissed from the service.
That is why Meredith's application
for reinstatement was found to have
no force, for he utterly failed to show
that he was innocent of the charge of
having allowed the noted procuress to
walk out of his hands.

The tornado struck the city last evening
when most of the residents were at sup
ppr, cutting a clean path one mile long j
and COO feet wide through the residence
section and leaving death and destruction
in its wake. Buildings were demolished
or twisted apart and scattered to the four
wind: A conservative estimate places
the aggregate amount of damage at $200,
--fv>o the individual losses ranging from
Jl'iu to $8000.
The wind played many pranks and there
were some miraculous escapes. J. T.
Coonfield and his family were eating sup
per when the tornado struck their home.
The house was picked up and carried
across a slough and dumped up against
a hill. The building was not completely
wrecked, and the family escaped without
anything worse than a severe shaking up.
Houses on both sides of the residence of
W. M. Hull were blown into splinters,
but the wind was content with twisting
his house off its foundation and leaving
it Intact. The house was in the center of
the district, and it was used as a hospital
after the inn.
P. J. Rleger, a prominent attorney, ran
right into the path of the storm to save
his family, who were helpless with fear.
Dashing into the house, he carried his
â wife and children into the cellar. As he
stumbled into the place of safety the
building was leveled to the ground. He
was just in tin to save his loved ones
and is happy, although his loss by the
cyclone amounts to J3OOO.
Among the first of the buildings wrecked
was the $10,000 residence of Mrs. Hensley,
iii the southeastern part of the city. Not

For a time the people in that portion of
the town not touched by the storm were
stricken, but as soon as they recov- 1
â red their wits they immediately went to
the aid of their unfortunate townsmen.
'alls were sent to surrounding towns and
were quickly responded to. The wounded
were carried to the homes of hospitable
people in the neighborhood. Twenty-five
doctors from Moberly, Macon and other '
points along the line of the Wabash rail- :
road came to Kirksville last night in re
sponse to urgent calls for medical assist
ance. The visiting doctors co-operated
with the local physicians and the work of
caring for the wounded was expedited.
Hundreds of persons visited tue stricken '
district ot-day and a large number of 1
=ightseers came from neighboring towns.
The special officers would not allow any '
one without authority to go through the
ruined district. Kirksville streets have
l>een hub deep in mud ever since the first '
-pring shower, and the rain that followed
the tornado has made them impassable
for wagon?. This state of affairs hinders
ihr> work of gathering the dead and makes
the compilation of the great list of victims
Impossible for the time being. All busi
ness has been enued, and the only j
stores open for business are those dealing |
in drugs.
The scenes of the rescuers were horri- |
fying. An aged man and his wife :
had evidently been eating L.eir evening
meal when the cyclone struck and carried
their home away. They had clasped each
other in their arms and were found dead j
i>n the ground some distance away from '
where their home had been. No trace of
their house has been seen. Several of the i
dead were found in spots which would I
have been the center of the house had the
building not been blown to splinters. This
was the case with Mrs. Panscnott, who â 
suffered a most terrible death. Her head
l< ss body, with its arms clasping her life- |
less baby, was discovered on the floor of
tho house, which was left as a razed plat- ,
form in an open field. The head was as !
nicely severed as if it had been done with
a knife and was about twenty feet dis
tant. Two children had been lying in a
cot in one of the destroyed homes.
The house was carried away, but the
floor of the first story and the founda
tion were left, the wind lifting the house
from the sills and tumbling it a long dis
tance. The cot on which the little ones
lay was lifted up with them on it and
carried fully a block and a half and set
down on the ground. Neither of the chil
dren was hurt. Along the railroad track
between tho depot and lloggs Crossing,
a distance of a half mile, was a mas of
debris. The wrecks of many homes were
scattered along there and tho trees v.- re
tilled with hodclothing and other house
hold goods.
Mayor Noonan, assisted by a number of
citizen opened an Intelligence bureau at
noon. Messengers were sent through the
stricken district in search of persons who
were known to have resided in that sec
tion of the town. Every citizen ma it
his duty to report at the bureau of in
formation all cases of death, injury and
of persons missing. In this way some
little degree of order was brought out of
chaos. - â¢
At 2 o'clock it was said at headquar- j
ters that the Injured so far numbered
seventy-four, with about a dozen cases !
which arc sure to result fatally. This list \
is not complete, however.
There are two undertakers in Kirks
ville. and together they had all they
could do taking care of the dead. A
meeting of citizens, at which the Mayor
presided, was held this afternoon at "the
opera-house. Committees were appointed
to systematize the work of clearing away
the debris, and finding shelter for the
homeless ones and raising funds for their
temporary care. The i are at least 1000
homeless people in Kirksville to-night.
The freaks of the storm were many,
but one of the strangest occurrences was
the finding of a crooning baby out in a
field far from any house. The child was
scarcely scratched. No one has called for
the baby and it has not been identified. i
Another baby was found where it had
been bruised to death by the violent wind.
This baby also has not been identified.
Among the horrors of the storm was
the finding of the corpse of a 16-year-old !
girl, with a two-by-four scantling driven
through her body. The body of a little
child was found, with the limb of a tree
sticking through its neck. A citizen was
found after the storm standing over the
body of his dead wife. The head had i
been crushed and severed from the body.

LOS ANGELES, April 28.â Four
days have elapsed since Hillary
S. Starr, the young superinten
dent of the Oak Knoll ranch,
near Pasadena, suddenly disap
peared, under circumstances strongly
pointing to murder. Up to a late hour
to-night no solution of the tragical
mystery has been found, although offi
cers have continued the search and
have investigated every clev.\
Wilson Lako, the historic body of
\ water at the base of the old Mission
Mill, has been thoroughly dragged for
1 the body of the missing man, but noth
| ing was found that would lead to the
' conclusion that his captors or abduct
i ors had attempted to conceal the crime
: by throwing Starr's body Into the lake.
1 While the public belief In Pasadena
still leans to the theory of murder and
i robbery, there are those, including
; some officers, who incline to the opinion
\ that Starr may have taken this sen
| sational method to voluntarily disap
pear from his home.
As Stan- is admitted to have
possessed a gloomy disposition, he may
| have wandered away while temporarily
insane, but his friends contend that his
â  family relations were of the best, and
j that Starr would hardly smash his own
lantern and have a buggy concealed In
j the. woods and use it to spirit himself
away.

ANCIENT BED OF
KLONDIKE RIVER
Bench Claims Yield Big
Returns.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
TACOMA, April John Galbralth of
' Sturgis, S. D., who returned to-day from
i Dawson, was one of the most fortunate
i prospectors who went to the Klondike
I last year. He brought out $60,000 In
drafts, over $1600 In cash and several
pounds of nuggets, which represent the
net proceeds of his single bench claim
! during the past year.
Galbraith started north a year ago, ac
| companied by J. C. Brown of Montana.
i They reached Dawson In May. After rest
! Ing for a few days they started to pros
pect. Ten miles out they spent the night
at a cabin. The next morning they com
menced panning gravel on the hillside
above them. Coarse colors were found
In each pan, and they decided to ' sink
holes at a place where 20 cents to the pan
was realized. Each took up a claim, Gal
bralth getting a Discovery claim of 250 by
1000 feet. a stampede followed their re
cording and the hillsides were staked for
miles. Galbraith ran a tunnel, which is
In ISO feet.
As they progressed inward the frozen
gravel became richer, indicating they
had struck the ancient bed of Klondike
River. Galbralth's tunnel has produced
over $100,000 in gold. He employs twelve
men. This, together â  with the royalty,
1 cost of wood and other expenses, reduced
, his net proceeds to $63,000. The unwashed
gravel now taken out will yield $25,000 at
the June clean-up. Galbraith estimates
the gold-bearing value of his claim at
$1,500,000.
Brown has remained to look after their
1 interests. His claim is producing almost
I equally well. Galbraith's claim has made
! the best record as a producer of any
I bench claim.
The shooting of a Katha Indian named
John by his hunting companion, Alexan-
I der, last month caused excitement among
the Dawson Indians. Alexander was trail
ing moose when he noticed a movement
In the bushes and fired, thinking an ani
mal was coming toward him. The bullet
pierced both of John's lungs and he died
immediately. Rev. Mr. Totty, a mission
ary, saved Alexander from arrest.
Two Circle City miners recently fought
a duel over a pretty dressmaker. The
principals were named Thompson and
Sullivan. One man had a revolver and '
the other a knife. Sullivan loved Annie
Blank and was visiting at her cabin.
Thompson came along and demanded ad
mittance. When refused he started to
break down the door. Doors being expen- !
sive at Circle City Sullivan decided it was I
I cheaper to open the door and stab Thomp-
I son. He undid the fastenings and plunged
: his long dirk knife into Thompson's lung.
The latter drew his revolver and shot
Sullivan through the body. Both were
expected to die.

F;>ec!al Dispatch to The Call,
PORTLAND, Or., April 28.â RoV. David
Clayborne Garrett, reotor of Trinity Epls- j
copal Church of this city, has accepted
the rail from St. Luke's BptÃcopal I
Church of Run Francisco. At a meeting

resignation, tn take effect on September j
1. About two months rko the call from ;
flt. Luke'B was extended to Dr. Garret t, |
but he decided he could not leave the
work which he had entered upon here.
After further deliberation and the adjust- j
ment of church affairs here he decided to j
accept the call from San Francisco.
Mr. Garrett was born in Burlington,
In w.i. on December 23. 1857. He was edu
cate,! in the public schools of Burlington,
Griswold College at Davenport and Har
vard University, where he received the
degree of A. I?. In W2. In ISS3 he was or
dalned rector of Trinity Church, Daven
port, where be served for Beven years;
until 1890, during which time hf> aided in!
building up the parish from a small con
ition to a larpe and flourishing
church, clearing off all indebtedness,
erecting- a rectory and doubling the num
ber nf -communicants. In 1890 he was
c.""ii< (i to Seattle, whore he duplicated
Ills work in Davenport. While changes j
occurred In the pastorate of very church j
In Seattle, Mr. Garrett* remained through
all the "hard" times, declining numerous
calls to larger parishes. After three years
he was railed to go back to his old par
ish in Davenport and also to Christ
Church Cathedral, New Orleans.
In ISM he was elected rector of Trinity
Church, Portland, but felt it his duty to ,
decline. The next spring, through his |
efforts, the handsome new St. Mark's i
Church was built Then Trinity Church
of this city sent a second call, having
gone a Whole year without a rector, and
Mr. Garrett came to Portland. Princi
pally through Mr. Garretfs efforts the
debt of |40(000 on the parish was virtually
raised by the issue of bonds.
Mr. Garrett was married in ISS6 to Miss
Lillian S. Elmer of. Boston, and has three
children.

--000, and has proved a fine investment. For
the five years since its first crop was man- !
ufactured it has yielded dividends yearly I

KYTKA QUALIFIES AS
AN EXPERT ON INK
WILLOW'S, April 28.â The Murdoch:
note case has been at a standstill as re
gards evidence for the past two days, as
the attorneys have been arguing on the
admissibility of testimony. Th< defense
put a witness on the .-nv.nl Wednesday to
prove that William Murdock had plenty
of money when the $100,000 note was given.
This question has been argued for the
past two days and submitted to the
court yesterday at B o'clock. This morn
ing Judge I'irkey sustained the objection
which shuts out all such < vidence.
The defense then called Ames, the hand
writing â¢ â¢xpvrt. The affidavit of Samuel
Murdock, which was signed before Jus
tire of the Peace Klrkpatrick In 1878 was
shown to Ames and i, ( attempted to
prove that the affidavit was made years
later than 1878. Under cross-examination
Ames said he could not tell the age of
the document by its appearance. It was
not written within the past month, but
might have been written within a year.
Expert Kytka was recalled and quali
fied as an expert on ink. He was aski d
how different inks looked when photo
graphed. He had photographed the affi
davit under discussion aiid explained that
ink which had yellowed by age t"<ik On a
much darker hue. Up said the photograph
showed the ink was d'luted. Black ink
which was undiluted would appear thirty
years fmm now a perfectly solid and
even line, while in the diluted ink in the
instrument there appeared ragged lines
caused by the coloring matter n< wing
out to the edges and lodging there. There
was not a single instance in the M"i docu
ments admitted in evidence where dilut
ed ink had been used' except in the case
of the affidavit.

FOR CLEVELAND

settled upon former President Grover Cleveland as the occupant of the
chair. It is even said that the donor of the fund had Mr. Cleveland in
mind, and that the place has been created in order that he may be
formally attached to the university in which he has shown such a lively
interest since he last left the White House.
As the university already has a professorship of jurisprudence and
politics, which is filled by Professor Woodrow Wilson, there was some
speculation as to the purpose in creating a new chair for general poli
tics until Mr. Cleveland's name was mentioned in connection with the
place. It was explained that the new professorship would embrace in
ternational law, political science, diplomacy and the fundamental prin
ciples of government. The field to be covered will be supplementary
to that covered by Professor Wilson.

SAX QUENTIN PRISON. April 2S.â
"Hank Voges," one of the peculiar
characters at the prison, who bad been
employed for several years as a guard,
was forced to resign his position to-day
by Warden Hale. Voges was formerly
the Town Marshal at Randsburg and was
imported by the warden at a tim._- wh< 11
Hale appeared to think nobody suitable
to officiate as guard unless lie were a
"gunÂ»flghter." Voges' feats of tongue
h.avi long been the marvel of the prison.
It was only a short time ago that he
alarmed the whole prison and turned
things topsy turvy by emptying his re
volver at a dog in the belief that it was
an .. pium planter. Voges was discovered
Bleeping at his post last night and ad
mitted his fault when brought before the
warden to-day. Voges was permitted to
resiun instead Of suffering- the ignominy
of a discharge.

ment house at the bluff belonging to

SINGLES TOURNAMENT
AT CALIFORNIA CLUB
Three days of tournament tennis will
crowd the courts of the California Tennis
Club this afternoon, Sunday and Monday.
The club has arranged a singles class
tournament and, judging from the en
tries, some very close matches will be
seen before the finals are reached.
In the preliminary matches the best two
out of three will decide; and in the finals
the best three out of five sets. The en
trance fee has been fixed at 50 cents. En
tries will not close until the time for
drawing. Up to date the following have
signed their names to the entry list: R.
J. Davis. Werner Stauf. Harry Weihe,
Duval Moore, R. W. Bliven, Dr. C. B.
Root. H. W. Walter, J. A. Code and Rob
ert N. Whitney. Suitable prizes will be
given the winner and runner-up.
John D. Spreckels Jr. has returned to
the courts. On Thursday morning he
played three hours, from 7 to 10, with
Professor Joe Daily. The latter proved
too much for SprecKels. defeating him in
three straight sets. Walter Hobart will
renew his playing. His first trial will be
made this afternoon.
Cyclers" Outing.
The Ynpemite . Wheelmen's f'luh will
hold a mnonlipht run to San Jose this
evening. Cyclers will moet ;tt Fruitvale,
taking the last boat from this city.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, SATURDAY, APRIL 29, 1899.
ST. BERNARDS WILL BE
A DOG-SHOW FEATURE
Cfoarleg Celebrated fiolden Lion.
OX Wednesday next, when the annual show of the San Francisco Kennel
Club opens at Mechanics' Pavilion, there will be benched a collection of
dogs as high in quality as were ever exhibited in^ the United States. In
number of entries It gives way to New York alone, exceeding all the other
largo cities on the Eastern dog show circuit. Five hundred and sixty-nine dogs,
representing almost every known breed, will be shown.
The St. Bernards will he better represented than heretofore. The total entry
is ninety-eight, as against forty-six last year and fifty-nine In IW. This increase
is ascribed to the energy of the members of the St. Bernard Club of California,
who are untiring In their efforts to foster the interest of fanciers in the "Holy
Breed.'' Golden Lion, from the kennel of Charles Newman, is a prominent candi
date for honors in this class.
That the specialty clubs are efficient in the execution of their purposes may
l>e Been in the mastiff entry this year, which reaches the record number of thirty
three. New York only benched sixteen of this breed, Chicago and Boston even
loss: so this large entry may be justlj regarded as th<- result of the work of
the Pacific Mastiff Club, whose members show no less than fourteen of the dogs.
In "1W only thirteen mastiffs were benched and In 1897 twenty-five. A pretty
spectacle will be a bunch of puppies sired by Chino Don, out of Lola, of which
Mrs. J. P. Norman was the owner. Mrs. Norman show-s two of the pupsâ Lola
and Hebe Montea. Messrs. I'ick. Murray and Lincoln show three dogsâ Bu<_-no,
Newman and Don Montez.
The Fox-Terrier Club is another instance in point. Tn ISO 7 the club was in a
flourishing condition and fox-terrier entries numbered forty-four. In 1898 the
club languished, and entries sank to twenty-six. This year, owing to J. B. Mar
tin's energy ami power of organization and Mr. Hickman's liberal pecuniary and
moral support, the club has been effectively resuscitated and the entries have
risen to fifty-one, <>n which splendid result Messrs. Hickman and Martin, presi
dent and secretary of the club, have every reason to congratulate themselves.
The California Collie Club has practically effaced Itself from recognized dog
dom by joining an unimportant local organization in opposition to the American
Kennel Club. This is to be regretted, as some of the members of the club are
am"iig the most enthusiastic dog fanciers of the CO&St.
Hull-terriers are another breed that show a decided increase over previous
years, Inasmuch as forty dogs are shown, against thirty-four In l^ l^ and twen
ty-three in 1887, indicating a steady and substantial gain in popularity.
SAUSALITO WILL
BE GAY TO-DAY
The San Franciscos Will Op. a
the Yachting Sea
son.
The oldest yacht club on the bay of San
Francisco will to-day open the season at
Sruisalito. The clubhouse and ferry boats
will be gayly decorated with flags, and the
yachts lying at th.c club anchorage will
dress ship at 1:30 p. m. The yachts and
clubhouse will be open for the reception
of visitors during the afternoon, md at
6:30 p. m. a supper will be served to the
members and visiting yachtsmen.
At 7:30 p. m. sharp the tug Sea King
will leave Mission street wharf 1, having
on ' board the Invited guests from this
city. On arriving at the club wharf atS
o'clock she will be saluted by guns fired
on the yachts. During the evening there
will be dancing, fireworks and illumina
tions of the yachts. The tug Sea King
will leave the clubhouse at 11:30 to bring
back guests to the city. The regular ferry
boats will also run, the last leaving the
city for Sausalito at 6:30 p. m., and the
last returning to the city from SausaJito
at 10:5(i p. m. The committee in charge
of the arrangements for opening day con
sists of Dr. a. VV. McKenzie, Frank L.
Platt, M. H. Hernan, J. R. Savory, L. Q.
Havens and H. B. Holbrook.
"Colors" will be made at S a. m. to-mor
row, and owners will report on board the
flagship Cygnus at 10:30. A little later,
on signal from the commodore, the fleet
â will cruise in squadron from Sausalito to
the city front, accompanied by visiting
yachts. Here th<; San Francisco squad
ron will be joined by the yachts of the
California and Corinthian clubs, and the
combined fleet will cruise along the water
front, and later, when the flood tide Bets
in, will square away for Raccoon Straits,
San Pablo Bay and Vallejo.
Commodore Allen M. Clay has issued
the following orders: The boats of the
California Yacht Club will leave moor
ings on Saturday, April 29, at 3;45 p. m.,
and cruise to Sausalito, where they will
anchor off the San Francisco Yacht Club
bouse and take "colors" from gun on
Commodore T. L. Hill's flagship. On Sun
day morning yachts will dress ship at
"colors" and thereafter will follow Com
modore T. L. Hill's signals. In company
with the Corinthian fleet the California
yachts will join the opening cruise of the
San Franclscos to Vallejo and will return
on Monday
In general order No. 2 Commodore Allen
M. Clay thanks the committees which bad
the opening night entertainment on April
22 In charge, and congratulates them on
the success which attended their labors.
He also expresses his appreciation of the
cordial and unanimous support given to
him by yacht owners on the occasion of
the first squadron cruise on Sunday, April
23. A telephone has been placed in the
house of the California Yacht Club.
The South Bay Yacht Club opened the
season last Sunday and will hold the first
regatta of the season on Sunday, May 7.
The programme for ISM Includes cruises
to Redwood City, Napa, the drawbridge
and Belmont Slough, three ladles' days,
two clambakes and several afternoon
sails. During the Decoration day holl
days arid again during the Admission day
holidays the squadron will cruise to San
Francisco. Members of the club are wel
come on board any of the boats on the
days mentioned in the programme, and)
are requested to notify Secretary F L,
Machefert when they wish to attend a
cruise. On ordinary occasions members
should bring lunch with them, but on
certain days the club will supply refresh
ments.
The Encinal Yacht Club will give an
opening entertainment toward the end of
May.

ALL ABOUT FISHING.
Where Striped Bass Can Be Caught.
Opening of Lake Pilarcitos to
Anglers.
Thp ."trip*.) i. .train rÂ»t In mi
arance in the Oakland estuary. To
reach the grounds anglers should take the
that leave on the hour by the broad
gauge and got off at <irand station, Ala
meda.
The fishing is to the north of the sta
tion and distant about (00 yards. The best
time for bass fishing is during the In
coming tide, but frequently they feed at
low water. The lure* which have proved
jful are clams and live or fresh
shrimps. As to the kind of tackle best
adapted for the sport. Henry Skinner, the
well-known By caster, will sive anglers
full particulars.
During the early part of the week sev
eral gplendid bass were tak.'ii by Al Wil
son, w. F. McFarland and General Crack
ii'-il of this city, and also resident anglers
of Alameda. Young Cracknel! hpoked and
landed a 21t6-pound basa on a B-ounce
bamboo rod last Monday. Tin- tish gave
the lad a tight which lasted thirty min
utes. Last rhursday Professors Marcus
and .Terry tried their luck for bass, but
owing: t.r a strong and cold wind that
blew from the west during the afternoon
and discolored the water th<Mr !u<-k was
po 'i. Terry, however, succeeded In land-
Ing two Immense stingarees, one which
weighed 56% pounds. Although he failed
to hit the stripes, he enjoyed the sport ..f
Btingaree fishing immensely and will soon
try it again. John Butler has wagered
"the decapitation" of his "tule-chops"
it John Lemmers' imp. rial mustache
that he will land a 60-pound stingai
Btriped bass Inside of five minutes. The
challenge has created considerable talk in
fishing oircle? and the contest will be
h-ld in the early part of next week, prob
ably Tuesday.
The Spring Valley Water Company will
soon Issue permits to anglers who desire
to tish In Lake Pilarcitos. lmt under no
consideration will Lake Ban Andreas he
opened to anglers this Beason.
A meeting Of the Supervisors r.f Marin
County and all sportsmen who are Inter
ested In fishing and shooting: in Marin
County will be held on Saturday. May fi,
in the San Rafael Courthouse, commenc
ing at 1 O'clock. The object Of the meet
ing will he to discuss the proposed ordi
nance for the protection of fish and game
In the county. All sportsmen are Invited
to be present and express their views.

RELEASED ON
STRAW BONDS
AND ABSCONDED
Harry Wilson Again
at Liberty.
CLERK KELLY IN EVIDENCE
COrSTERNATIOk AT POLICE
HEADQUARTERS.
Descriptions of the Suspected Stran
gler Are Sent Broadcast Through
out the ~!tate for Purposes
of Identification.
The facility with which notorious crimi
nals are enabled to escape punishment for
their crimes was agui.i exemplified yes
terday in the case of Harry Wilson, the
negro who is suspected of being the man
who strangled two disreputable women
three years ago and robbed a number of
other women.
The detectives had been searching for
evidence against Wilson on the more seri
ous charges, but on Thursday it was de
cided to book him on three charges of
petty larceny so that hÂ»* could be kept
in prison pending the investigations on
the other suspected crimes. It was not
supposed that he could feet bonds for his
release and when it was known yesterday
morning that he had been released at 9:20
o'clock Thursday nlghi on $900 bonds ac
cepted by Clerk Kelly of Judge Graham's
court the worst was t\:ared and there was
consternation at police headquarters.
This was intensified whf-n it became
known that the cases had been called In
Judge Graham's court and Wilson had
failed to answer to his n;'.me.
Chief I>ei:s and Captain Seymour at once
saw Judge Graham and a bench warrant
was Issued for Wilson's arrest and his
bonds were increased to $2wu on each of
the three charges. The Chief wired all
over the State a description of Wilson
and asking for his arrest, while Detectives
Ed Gibson, Wren and McMahon were de
tailed with other men to search the city
for him in the hope that he was still
here. His description as wired is: Harry
Wilson, colored; thief; aged 39; born in
Kentucky; height 5 feet 6^ inches; build
good; weight 153 pounds, hair negro; com
plexion copper. Remarks: Brown coat
and vest: light trousers; soft gray shirt;
soft black hat."
Bond Inspector Newman was sent for
by the Chief ami he went to Clerk Kelly
j and saw the bonds. He at once declared
| that they were not worth the paper on
â  which they wore written, and subsequent
investigation proved that he was correct.
The bondsmen were E. Riley, an ex-dep
uty sheriff, and Thomas EL Cosgrove, 312
Howard street. Riley qualified on a one
! fifth Interest in a 50-vara lot of land
bounded by Devlsadero, Pulton, Grove
and Scott streets, valued at $8000 and un-
Incumbered. Cosgrove qualified on "two
lots in the Railmad Homestead Associa
tion, four carts, sixteen horses, three
buggies and eight wagons, valued at 11500
and unincumbered." Neither of the two
men is nn th assessment roll.
Clerk Kdly explained that Attorney J.
X. K. Wilson and the two sureties called
at his house Thursday night and Wilson
assured him that the sureties were all
right. He accordingly accepted the bonds
and Issued orders for the negro's release.
He claims that. he was compelled to ac
â¢ .pi the bonds by virtue of his office.
Bond Inspector Newman declares that
Attorney Wilson knew that Riley was not
acceptable as a bondsman, as hf- had
warned him on a former occasion of that
fact.
thief Lees, when he learned of all th*
Circumstances, was furious and emphati
cally declared that he had been bunkoed
and that the release of Wilson on straw
bonds was a gross outrage. Hr said ho
was powerless In the matter, as the clerks
of the courts were appointed by the Board
of Supervisors. He Instanced the fact
that it was through th.> acceptance of a
straw bond by Clerk Kelly that "Austra
lian Eddie,' 1 the notorious pickpocket was
enabled u> defeat the ends of justice, and
â¢ ' : ' It Was limr some action was
tak'ii to put a stop to that sort of thine
It is probable that the matter will be
taken before the liraml Jury to see If In
dictments cannot be obtained against
Riley and Cosgrove for perjury in ralsolv
swearing that they owned property which
they do not own. There have been so
many oasr-s recently of straw bonds that
some drastic measures must be taken to
give these men a lesson.
The Police .In. lees had a consultation
on the subject, but came to the conclu
sion they had no jurisdiction over their
clerks after the adjournment of the
courts, and could do nothing. Captain
Seymour prepared a list of prisoners re
! on bonds by the clerks of the
courts between the hours of n p m and
6 a. m.. since the beginning of this month
and the number reached thirty-five \\*
pointed out that according to* order No
2p passed by the Board of Supervisors
April ;>. 1893. clerks of the Police Courts
can only accept bonds in their respective
court rooms or office attached thereto be
tween the hours of 9 a. m. and 4pm
and that they shall alternate weekly in
the evenings from 7:80 to m o'clock, ex
cept Sundays and holidays when the
hours are 10 a. m. till 2 p. m. It is speci
fically stated that they shall not accept I
sureties on bonds except within the
places and the times prescribed. ,\s a
result orders have been posted in all po
lice Btations to accept no orders of re
lease from clerks of the Police Courts In
the future after 10 o'clock at night

At their last meeting the Alameda Boat
Club elected the following new members-

THE NEWS FROM MflNlLfl.
ANOTHER illustration of the superiority of the
special news service of The Call and the New
York Herald was given yesterday in the dis
patches from Manila announcing that Luna, chief of
staff of Aguinaldo, had sent an officer to our lines
to ask a cessation of hostilities.
That important item of news was sent to this coun
try exclusively by The Call-Herald service. In New
York it was published by the Herald only. The dif
ference in point of time between that city and San
Francisco enabled the Examiner to obtain it from
New York alter the Herald was on the street, and it
was therefore published in this city by that paper, but
without proper credit.
The importance of the news in this case renders it
a notable illustration of the effe^tiveneos of The Call-
Herald service, but it was by no means a rare per
formance. In fact hardly a week has passed since
the war with Spain began without furnishing some
proof of the superiority of our news gathering agency.
The Call and Herald, it will be remembered, were first
to get a report of the battle of Manila from an eye
witness, and we are now the first to give news of the
suit for peace on the part of the insurgents. To main
tain so effective a service of course require? a consid
erable expenditure of energy and money, but in the
end it pays better than Creelman fakes or stealing
news.
MR MUNTINGTON'S SPEECH-
A'OD speech in its way was that of Collis P.
Huntington at the Southern Pacific banquet,
but the way was wrong. Instead of moving for
ward to a discussion of the best means of promoting
the welfare of people now living in California, it
tended mainly to a consideration of the problem of
getting more people to live here. It is clearly not an
increase in the prosperity of California, but an in
crease in the number of persons who are subject to
the traffic taxes of the Southern Pacific, that interests
the gentleman.
"What California needs to-day," he said, "is more
le. What you merchants need is more customers
tc buy your goods." Then he uttered an epigrammatic
statement having all the dignity of an axiom: "The
only way you can get them is by having them here."
To accomplish the filling of the long felt want Mr.
Huntington suggested the importation of Finns.
"One little colony of hard-working, economical, vir
tuous people like the Finns," he said, "would leaven
the whole lump, for their example would be an in
citement to thousands, who, seeing their good work
and their good fortune, would strive to share it."
â¢le will dispute the desirability of increasing our
population, nor will there be any objection to an im
portation of Finns, provided they come to the State
with enough of money and skill to render them self
supporting from the start without crowding the labor
market and depressing the rate of wages. That, how
ever, is a comparatively minor issue afpresent. Cali
fornians are more interested just now in questions
of transportation charges than of immigration from
Finland. Had Mr. Huntinijton made his speech on
freight rates it would have been no doubt just as much
applauded at the banquet board and far more accept
able to the public.
Were the industries and the commerce of California
relieved from the burdens of excessive freight charges
there would be no need of applying to any foreign
land for immigrants. The natural riches of the State
are such that if it were not for the artificial.handicap
put upon progress by the railroad monopoly the pros
perity which would attend all its industries would
soon attract to our cities and to our fields a large
number of enterprising young Americans from all
parts of the East.
Despite all the Interstate Commerce Commission
has done, despite all the efforts of our merchants and
manufacturers, there exist ample reasons for believing
our trade is grossly discriminated against by the great
transcontinental lines. Mr. Huntington holds a po
tent position in railroad circles. He has proven him
self in many a conflict to be able, adroit and bold.
Californians are therefore justified in thinking that
if on this issue he fought as hard for the interests of
the State as. he has been known to fight against them
nt times we would now have a much more favorable
freight rate, not only on his line, but on all trans
continental lines. It is for that reason his speech at
the banquet will be regarded as something of a mis
take. He spoke on the wrong subject.
THE APPOINTMENT OF SENATORS.
THE country has been startled by a news de
spatch, which has gone for three days uncontra
dicted, that a treaty has been made whereby
Quay, is to be seated in the Senate on appointment by
the Governor, and that in return for the executive in
fluence which has committed a majority of Senators
to thft plan the State of Pennsylvania will support
President for renomination.
As a feature in the flux and change with which our
institutions are threatened, the subject of appointment
of Senators by Governors should be thoroughly dis
cussed and understood.
The abhorrent word "Treason" is creeping into
common use to characterize men who discuss the al
leged principles of our Government, but we assume
that an examination of the constitutional provisions
in reference to the Senate may yet be carried on with
out incurring the charge of being a traitor.
The constitution, article I, section 3, provides:
"The Senate of the United States shall be composed
of two Senators from each State, chosen by the Leg
islature thereof, for six # years, and each Senator shall
have one vote. Immediately after they shall be as
sembled in consequence of the first election they shall
be divided as equally as may be into three classes.
The seats of Senators of the first class shall be va
cated at the expiration of the second year, of the sec
ond class at the expiration of the fourth year, and of
the third class at the expiration of the sixth year, so
that one-third may be chosen every second year; and
if vacancies happen, by resignation or otherwise, dur
ing the recess of the Legislature of any State, the
executive thereof may make- temporary appointments
until the next meeting of the Legislature, which shall
then fill such vacancies."
Article V provides that "No State, without its con
sent, shall be deprived of its equal suffrage in the
Senate."
It would seem that there can be no two opinions
as to the origin and limitation of a Governor's juris
diction over the appointment of a Senator. The plain
language of the constitution seems to settle that:
"I* vacancies happen, by resignation or otherwise,
during the recess of the Legislature of any State, the
executive thereof may make temporary appointments
until the next meeting of the Legislature, which shall
then fill such vacancies."
Ever since 1793 from time to time the exercise of this
executive jurisdiction has been discussed in the Sen
ate. In many cases that have arisen the discussion has
been upon the cause of the vacancy and a legal con
struction of the term "happen." It has been argued
that executive jurisdiction appears and a Governor
may appoint only if a Senator during his term re
signs, dies, is expelled or by incapacity through in
sanity becomes incompetent and cannot even resign.
that being an act requiring sanity. In this view of it
the set and fixed term of the Senatorial office is not
v happening. Its beginning and end are fixed by
law. Senator \\'hite"s term, for instance, did not
"happen" to end on March 3 last, nor did the result- J
ing vacancy "happen." Its occurrence was fixed by
law. What "happens" is defined by all English
lexicographers to be that which comes unexpectedly.
But passing by the discussion of the right of a Gov
ernor to appoint when a vacancy has not happened,
but has come about by the expiration of the term
fixed by law, the Senate has never heretofore varied
from the rule that the Governor's jurisdiction arises
only when the vacancy occurs in the recess of the
Legislature. The constitution therein is so plain that
a layman, without knowledge of the rules of legal
construction, cannot err in its interpretation. When
the terms ot Senators White, Quay, Gray and Cannon
expired by law on the 3d of March, 1809, the Legis
latures of California. Pennsylvania, Delaware and
Utah were in session and not in recess.
The first case involving the jurisdiction of a Gov
ernor to appoint a Senator arose in Delaware. In ,
December, 1703. George Reade, Senator from Dela- j
ware, resigned. The Legislature met January, 1704, ,
?nd adjourned without electing a Senator. The Gov
ernor thereupon appointed Mr. Kensey Johns to the
vacancy. The Senate refused to seat him because the
Legislature had been in session since the vacancy oc
curred and its failure to elect did not give the Gov
ernor jurisdiction to appoint. In the Senate at that
time were several members who had been members '
of the convention which framed the Federal constitu- :
tion, and for that reason the decision of this case has :
always been given special weight and has been held to
warrant the application of the principle of stare de- j
ciÂ«is to the issue.
The next leading case, in which a full discussion ;
has been preserved, was that of Mr. Lanman of Con- I
necticut. His term expired March 3, iB->j. On March j
4 the Governor appointed him to succeed himself.
His credentials were dated February 28, and he ap
peared on March 4 to take his seat at a special ses
sion called by the President. He was refused his
seat, on the ground that: "The Legislature of the
State, by making elections themselves, shall provide
for all vacancies which may occur at stated and known
periods, and that the expiration of a regular term of
service is not such a contingency as is embraced in
the third section of the first article of the constitu
tion."
This decision was acquiesced in for fifty years, dur
ing which time Governors in nine States refused to 1
appoint to fill vacancies caused by expiration of regu
lar terms.
But in no case has the Senate seated an appointee
of a Governor when the Legislature has been in ses
sion and had a chance to fill the vacancy, as decided
in the Kensey Johns case.
If Quay is seated it will be in violation of that pre
cedent established by men who helped make the con
stitution, and no less in violation of the plain letter of ;
that instrument.
Against this there have been long arguments in
defense of the right of a State to full representation
in the Senate. In the constitutional sense the Legis
lature is the constituent of the Senator, who repre
sents the State. If the Legislature refuse to elect a
Senator it is in exactly the position of a citizen who !
neglects to vote. Nobody deprives him of his suf
frage He simply consents to deprive himself of it.
So the omission of a Legislature to elect a Senator is '
a State's consent to be deprived, temporarily, of its
full suffrage in the Senate.
It is so clear that the Governor has no jurisdiction
unless the vacancy occur in recess of the Legislature
that the alleged bargain to sent Quay is an agree
ment to violate the constitution. It is said in the \
same connection that Senator Jones, chairman of the
National Democratic Committee, and Senator But- j
ler, chairman of the Populist National Committee, are
pledged to vote for Quay's admission. It is not re
markable that they should do so, for they represent !
platforms which directly attack the constitution in its
vital parts. But we cannot believe that Republican
Senators can consent to such a palpable breach of
precedent and violation of the constitution which they j
have sworn to support and profess to revere. Nor i
can we believe that the President is a party to a bar
gain in the interest of his renomination. which, if ear
ned out. would deservedly condemn him to defeat.
The country is surely not in a mood to see the con
stitution trampled under foot for the benefit of Quay, !
Addicks and Dan Burns.
TIIEBMTRAK CISCO CALL, SATURDAY, APRIL 29, 1899.
(\ BUG IN THE CHARTER.
THE Freeholders, by whom the new charter was
framed, were carefully selected. As a body they
were intelligent and respectable citizens. They
included some lawyers of more than ordinary ability
and learning. However much the theory or the de-.
I tails of the municipal government for which they pro
! vide<l may have been criticized, their zeal and good
| faith have not heretofore been questioned.
But there is no doubt that, in some irysterious way
that has not been explained, these gentlemen were
literally bunkoed by the Pacific Telephone and Tele
graph Company, which is an oppressive, exacting and
odious monopoly. That corporation, within the
limits of this city and county, has charged and col
lected exorbitant rates for very ordinary service. It
is invested with extensive franchises and privileges,
exercised for years, for which no just equivalent has
been rendered, and its practice has uniformly been to
take everything it could get and treat the public not
enly with indifference, but with ostentatious con
tempt.
One of the principal objects before the Freeholders
in the production of the new charter was to bring
every corporation that owed its existence and its op
portunities for profit to the municipality within the
control of the Board of Supervisors, and especially
in the matter of rates and charges. The principle
that they were within the power of municipal regula
tion was accepted as fundamental, and, except as to
the telephone company, it was firmly applied. A brief
examination of the new charter will prove that this
particular exemption was not the result of acci
dent. Telephone companies are the subject of specific
provisions. Among the grants of power to the Su
pervisors enumerated in article 11, chapter 2, section
I, by the thirteenth subdivision, the right to control
the location and quality of appliances for telephonic
and telegraphic service to the city and county is in
cluded. By article VI, chapter i, section g, the erec
tion of poles and the laying of underground wires are
placed under the supervision of the Board of Public
Works. Telephonic and telegraphic service is desig
nated also as one of the public utilities that the muni
cipality is ultimately to acquire.
But this is all. It is sufficient to demonstrate that
the attention of the Freeholders was especially di
rected to the telephone monopoly, and, therefore, that
the plain discrimination in its favor was deliberately
planned. This proposition is also fortified by other
provisions of the new charter. Subdivision 14 of
section 1 of article 11, chapter 2. the very next to the
subdivision already mentioned, gives the Supervisors
the amplest authority to determine rates or compen
sation for the vise of water, heat, light or power.
Subdivision 27 of section 1 confers the right, as re
spects street railways, "to fix, establish and reduce,"
not to increase, "the fares and charges." Section 6
of the same chapter emphasizes this function of the
Board of Supervisors by enacting that it "shall at all
times have the power to regulate the rates of fare."
and the next section carefully applies the same rule
to franchises or privileges for transmitting electric
power or for lighting purposes.
It was quite natural for the Pacific Telephone and
Telegraph Company to support the new charter.
When that instrument was adopted its unexpected at
titude may have been attributed to a lofty motive.
But it was really prompted by the most arrant self
ishness. Like Mr. Huntington, the telephone mon
opoly went for everything in sight, knew a good thing
when it had it. and was not in business for its health.
In the rush of work toward the end of their labors the
Freeholders were certainly hoodwinked, for it is in
conceivable that any of their number used their posi
tion as a Trojan horse. -^
It would be interesting to trace the responsibility
foi this betrayal of the public interests in the midst
of an achievement that was expected to clothe the
municipality with the spotless robes of immaculate
purity.
The more important practical consideration, how
ever, is the remedy. There are many citizens, includ
ing lawyers, who believe that under the present State
constitution the rates of telephone companies can be
regelated. That is a matter for the prompt considera
tion of the Board of Supervisors, with the aid of the
City and County Attorney. But the public, which
looks to positive and intelligible law, free from legal
quibbles, demands that the necessary steps be taken
to amend the charter and frustrate the deep-laid
scheme of the telephone monopoly without a mo
ment's unnecessary delay. The process is slow at
best, but it should be speedily and effectively invoked.
'INTERNAL vigilance will undoubtedly be the
price of securing a competitive railway fran
â* chise on Geary street. If the Supervisors grant
the application of the Market street combine for per
mission to build an electric road on that thoroughfare
between Taylor street and Grant avenue they may give
away the entire subject. There is reason to
believe that the Market street company has not aban
doned its plan of securing a long franchise on Geary
street, free from the restrictions which will be thrown
around it by the new charter. It is understood that
the present franchise, which expires in 1902, will be
surrendered some time during the next three or four
months and that the present Board of Supervisors
will be asked to advertise for the sale of a privilege
upon the street, extending over the time limited by
the new charter, which is twenty-five years.
Inasmuch as the Market street company now has a
road upon the street in actual operation, it is in a p<%
sition to bid higher for a new franchise than any com
peting company, and if an award is made at this time
it will undoubtedly be able to prevent the introduc
tion of a competitor. The situation will be changed
somewhat if the sale of a franchise is postponed until
the- expiration of the present privilege. The new char
ter will then be in effect, and there wÂ»U be some en
couragement for a competing corporation to make a
bid, with a view to securing the privilege. At all
events, the attempt of the Market street company to
gobble up this street without reference to the rights,
of the people who reside and own pioperty on it
should be thwarted.
It will be dangerous for the Board of Supervisors
to grant any privileges to the proposed "corkscrew"
line upon Geary street. That line is designed to run
over a route formerly covered by what was known as
the Central Railway Company, whose franchise on
Geary street does not expire for many years to come.
The Central company did not have the privilege of
using electricity, and to permit at the present time
the use of electricity on Geary street from Taylor to
Grant avenue may and probably will jeopardize a con
tinuous franchise for that street. No competing com
pany will desire to build a road over a line partly oc
cupied by the combine's cars.
Eternal vigilance, as we have said, is the solution
of this difficulty. The Supervisors will be recreant to
their trust if they are fooled into giving away Geary
street to this grasping corporation.
A few pessimists are of the opinion, judging from
the name of the cruiser bearing the Samoan Commis
sioners to Apia, that their High Mightinesses are in
a fair way to Badger somebody.
