Mrs. Sabin Opens Her Home for the
Episcopal Old Ladies.
The reception ami musicals given vps
terday afternoon at the residence of Mrs.
John I. Sabin. 2528 California street, for
the benefit of the Episcopal Old Ladies'
Home, was both a financial and social
success. The beautiful home was crowded
during the afternoon and evening, and
special attention was paid to the excellent
musical programme. Miss Miller, pianiste.
Miss Millie Flynn. Frank Coffin and Dan
Gornam contributed to the afternoon
programme.
In the evening the choir of Grace
Church, under the direction of Professor
Holt, contributed the musical part of the
entertainment. Among the soloists were
Hnmpr Henley, Mr. vaccho and Henry
Holmes, the English violinist who has
lately created a marked "fnsation here.
The gem of the evening was the Minia
tures "in which pretty gtr s posed The
Pictures included "Autumn." by Miss Sa
bin "A Portrait Study " Miss Southack;
"Queen Louise of Prussia, by Miss Prin
dle: "Pompadour." by Miss <*$>**â¢ and
"Princess Postowka." by Miss Kline.

TO STOP A PAPERâ F. A. H.. Alex
ander Valley. Cal. If a publishing house
continues to send you a paper after the
time of subscription has expired, and you
; have notified the publishers that you no
. longer desire it and you refuse to receive
! it from the postmaster, then you cannot
be held responsible to the publishers.

A POINT IN CASINOâ G. G. and M.,
City. In playing casino, if A has six
points to go and B has five points, A
! makes cards, big casino and one ace,
> equal to six points, and B makes spades,
] little casino and three aces, A goes out
] first, as he has the first right to count by
â  reason of having cards. In this game the
! one who holds cards is always entitled to
â¢ count first, and if he has enough points
I to go out the other player or players need
not count.

A FIGHT FOR
BLOOD MONEY ON
COLUMBIA RIVER
Heavy Demands by
Boarding Masters.
CAPTAINS SEEKING MEN HERE
I
CREWS BEING SENT NORTH TO
MAN THE SHIPS.
! America Maru Arrives and Coptic
Sails for the â President
Farrell o*: the Pacific Coast
Company Here.
; ,
The trouble between the shipmasters
and boarding masters on the Columbia ;
River has been transferred in a measure :
to San Francisco. As stated in yester
day's Call, the boarding masters will not
accept the month's advance allowed by
law, but are demanding $100 "blood
money." The captain of the British ship [
Balmoral tried to escape paying the tax ''â 
by bringing nine men from San Francisco â 
to fill the vacancies in his crew, but he j
failed. The boarding masters were wait
ing for the men and enticed them away
as soon as they set foot on shore.
The venture of Captain Campbell of
the Balmoral was watched with a great
deal of interest, as seven other vessels
want crews, and their captains refuse to i
pay $1W for each sailor put aboard by the
boarding master. The vessels lying in i
the Columbia River and either loading or |
loaded for Europe are the Balmoral, How- j
ard D. Troop, Varana. British Princess, I
Wallacetown, Iverna and Marechal Su
chet.
Like the Balmoral, the Howard D. I
Troop is ready to sail and Captain Corn- j
ing wants seventeen men. He refused to j
pay the blood money, and he and the :
boarding masters had a lively alterca- I
tion, which resulted in the price of each j
sailor being advanced $12. Captain Corn- i
ing then determined to secure a crew :
from this port and the boarding masters
determined to balk him. ;
The master of the Howard D. Troop j
went to work very quietly and sent his |
chief officer and an agent to San Fran- i
Cisco to secure a crew of seventeen men. !
The mate had instructions not to stick at !
a few dollars' advance in wages, but to â 
bring back the men and so outwit the \
boarding masters. The latter were not
Idle, however, and as soon as the mate ]
and the agent started for this city Dick j
McCarron and two other boarding mas- !
ters were on their track. Thursday the |
mate of the Howard D. Troop secured a |
number of sailors and yesterday morn- |
Ing he started them for Astoria on the |
steamer State of California, in charge of !
the agent. On the same steamer went ;
one of the Columbia River boarding mas- j
ters. He says he will get to the ear of ;
the sailors, and that when Astoria is !
reached not one of the men will join the j
vessel.
Tuesday next the mate of the Troop
and the remainder of the crew will start
for Astoria on the Grace Dollar and on !
the same ship will go Dick McCarron and :
I the rest of the boarding masters. They |
will undoubtedly prevent the sailors from j
I joining the Troop, and in consequence thÂ»
i ship will have a big expense bill piled up |
j against her, and Captain Corning will be j
! no better off than he was before the fight
! began. He is a fighter, however, and as
Baifour. iuthrle & Co., Glrvin & Eyre, |
G. W. McNear and other large charterers I
of vessels are on his side, the battle is i
likely to be a bitter one. On th. next at
tempt to send men from here the chances
are that the vessel will be at anchor at
the mouth of the Columbia River and .1
tug will be there ready to take her to
sea. Then as soon as the steamer ar- ;
rives from San Francisco the sailors will .
be bundled aboard the tug and they will i
S be transferred to the waiting ship, which
will then put to sea.
The Japanese mail steamer America
i Maru arrived from the Orient yesterday
I afternoon. She brought up 116 cabin pas- i
sengers, the majority of whom came |
from Honolulu, and 223 Chinese in the j
steerage. It took a long time to transfer j
the Chinese to the quarantine station and I
It was after 5 p. m. before the steamer]
got to her dock. Shy was twenty-nine i
days from Hongkong, seventeen days ;
fro"m Yokohama and six days one hour
from Honolulu. During the early part of (
the Voyage the America Maru encoun- i
tered strong east-northeast winds and a
rough head sea and throughout the last
twenty-four hours .he had to buck a i
strong northwest gale and a high con- j
fused sea. - ' ' '. ' â , , i
The Occidental and Oriental Steamship j
Company's steamer Coptic sailed for the j
Orient yesterday. She carried a very ,
large cargo and a great many passengers. ;
Never in the history or the port has the j
freight and passenger traffic between San
Francisco and the Orient been as heavy
es it is at the present lime. |
Captain Charles Austin has taken com- |
mand of the schooner John G. North and
will take her to Hawaii. He replaces Cap
tain Christiansen, who retires. Captain
Austin is very popular in the sugar trade,
having been eight years mate of the Al
-1 den Besse and one year master of the
Maid of Orleans.
President J. Farrell of the Pacific Coast
Company arrived from Puget Sound last
Thursday on a tour of inspection. He
looked over all the company's steamers
yesterday and from here will go to Port
Harford, where he will inspect the San
Luis Obispo Railroad.
CYCLERS MUST NOT SCORCH.
Park Commissioners Will Not Allow
Fast Riding on Any of the
Drives.
The {'ark Commissioners, thoughtful of
their duty to the people who seek the pub
lic playground for recreation, have de-
Cided not to countenance fast riding of :
bicyclists on any of the driveways.
Representatives of the organized wheel
men have aKked that they be allowed to
ride at any rate of speed on the south I
(irlve, which skirts the children's? play
ground, them 'â¢ out by the baseball ground j
and around Strawberry Hill to the beach.
The C 'mmissioners, denied the request on '
the ground that ladieÂ« driving through the
nark often select the less frequented ',
drive, and fast riding would, sooner or !
later, result in an accident. The ocean
boulevard was thrown open to the wheel- j
m*n. They may ride over this at any I
rate of speed they can raise.

BOLD CROOKS
ROUNDED UP BY
THE POLICE

for Burglary.
I
STOLE VALUABLE BOOTY
ONE OF THE GANG A DESPERATE
EX-CONVICT.
The Men Are Accused of Entering [
Several Furnishing Goods
Houses on Kearny
Street.
During the past week several stores on
Kearny street have been entered -by a|
gang of burglars and valuable booty
taken.
The burglaries were reported to Cap- â¢
tain Bohen, who detailed Detectives Gib- 1
son. Wren and Dinan to arrest the dar- 1
ing crooks. Yesterday with the assist- I
ance of Policeman Engle, they succeeded ;
in capturing the gang.
Their names are John Sullivan, Fred
Watson, alias Mullen; Joseph Morgan, 1
Mike Joyce. F. B. Xewoauer and James
Hayes. Watson is an ex-convict, having'
served a term in San Quentln for burg
larj committed in this city.
On the night of April 14 last the gang
entered R. C. Hahn's furnishing goods
store at 133 Kearny street and got away
with about $im worth of silk handker
chief and neckties and fifty dollars in
money, which they found in the cash
drawers. An entrance was effected by
climbing through the transom of â  the
Stole.
\bout a week later they entered the ,
furnishing goods store of H. \V. Selig at,
218 Kearny street in a similar manner
and stole a miscellaneous assortment of
gloves, neckties and underwear, valued at
On the night of April 2H the gang en- ;
tered the California Clothing Company's
store at W Geary street and got away
with about $200 worth of stuff.
Convinced that Uic crimes were com
mitted by Watson and his gang, the
detective! started out to find them. By
dint of inquiry they learned that the men
Were living in a house on Minna stnvt.
neai Third, and they decided to make a !
â¢ t on them yesterday afternoon, j
While two of the officers covered the
house to prevent the gang from escaping
the others entered and going to the room ;
occupied by the crooks quickly burst
open th.^ door. They found Sullivan, Wat
son. Morgan and Joyce in the room,
9tandii g over s bed on which lay a large
portion of their booty. They offered no
resistance and were at once taken to the
City Prison, where their names were reg- ,
Istered on the "small" book.
Subs, fni' ntly the detectives found New- ;
bauer and Hayes on Market street, in the
act of trying to dispose of part of the
plunder stolen from Hahn's furnishing
foods store. They were also taken to po
headquarters and locked up in the
tanks. To-day the men will be formally
I ked on three charges of burglary
each. _____^^___â .

HIS REMAINS
TO BE EXHUMED
AND REBURIED
W. Tiedemann's Body
Hawked About.
NO RESPICT FOR THE DEAD
TROUPL^ BETWEEN CORONER
AND UNDERTAKER.
The Official Had the Remains In
terred by a Rival Firm ana jxietz
ler Secures a Singular
Order From Court.
Coroner Hill and Herman Metzler, an
undertaker, are having war over the
corpse of William Tiederrdr. â , who died
in the Prescott House on April J.,. On the
21st the body was buried in Laurel Hill
Cemetery from the California Undertak
ing Parlors, and on that fact hinges the
animosity of Metzler and his determina
tion to secure possession of the remains of
William Tledemann.
Metzler was called in by the clerk of the
Prescott House when Tiedemann was
found dead. He immediately notified the
Public Administrator, who took charge of
the effects of the deceased/amounting to
$5iÂ«W in bank and other personal property.
The Coroner was called later and the dep
uty carted the body to the California I n
dertaklng Parlors and refused Metzler in
formation as to its whereahouts. Metzler
then obtained an order from the Public
Administrator for possession of the body
for interment. He presented this to the
Coroner, who Informed him that he had
the absolute right to dispose of the body
for burial.
Metzler made implication yesterday be
fore Judge Murasky for the disinterment
of the body and a second burial from his
parlors. Judge Murasky granted the re
quest.
Metzler Is now armed with permits from
the Health Department to exhume the
body, which he intends to do this morning.
Coroner Hill says he will prevent it. He
telephoned to the Laurel Hill Cemetery
superintendent yesterday not to allow the
exhumation and he will be on hand to as
sert his authority.

The weather is generally fair over the Pacific
Coast, with some cloudiness In Southern Cali
fornia. Very little rain hits fallen west of the 1
Rocky Mountains. The pressure continues high i
over Oregon ami Washington and conditions are
favorable for somewhat warmer weather in the
interior Saturday.
The temperature as already risen over Ore
gon. Idaho and Northern Nevada. It is still
belnw the normal in iVntral and Southern Cali- '
fornia.

THE LONDON MARKET.
NEW YORK. April 28.â The Commercial Ad
vertiser's London financial cablegram says:
The market here had a general setback to-day,
partly due to vague rumors of Transvaal po
litical trouble, but mainly on account of the
closing of books before the holiday on Monday.
The inactivity in Americans defies description.
They were neglected and heavy throughout, ex
cept for slight New York support in Northern
Pacific and Erie. Copper shares are elapsed. j
Anaconda closed at 1- 15-18. Tintons opened at !
47 and closed at 4SV&.
Silver was very active, 2,000,000 ounces chang- !
ing hands. The buying was nearly all mdi- j
rectly from New York. It is reported that a j
bear dealer here is already in difficulties. The
morning price was 25%. It has since been 29-,*. :
and closed at 29 1-16. Two hundred thousand '
pounds In gold was taken for the Cape. It Is
expected that a parcel due to-morrow will go
to the Bank.

BRADSTREET'S FINANCIAL REVIEW.
NEW YORK, April 28.â Bradstreet's Finan
cial Review will say to-morrow: Speculation
this week developed narrow and Irregular tend
encies. The easing of the money market, ;
larger supplies of funds and a noticeable relax
ation of the disiwsition to reject Industrial
stocks as collateral had a favorable Influence,
which was partially overcome by the continued ;
uncertainty about the crops and the evident
â¢ falling off -In the public interest. The latter !
i fact was reflected by the much smaller volume
I of dally transactions and the comparatively
I irresponsive state of the market. The most
significant feature of . the week was the de
cided activity and strength of the bond list.
The recent change In the savings bank Invest
ment laws of New York anil Massachusetts, j
which opens to the savings Institutions of these j
States the bonds of dividend-paying railroads,
is to a large extent responsible for the move
ment in question, though this would only ac
count indirectly for the, in some cases, heavy <
appreciation In many issues of bonds . which \
have on the whole a semi-speculative position ;
In the market. The abundance of funds for
investment and the temporary diversion of pub- :
lie buying power from stocks to the bond list !
would seem also to be a factor in the matter, '
the effect, as usual In such cases, being to sus- '
tain the general range of stock quotations. The
market, however, was of an irregular kind.
The grangers were neglected and the same
applies to the Pacific stocks, while, on the other |
hand, the coal shares showed a tendency to j
appreciate on the better earnings which are
said to be rewarding the companies and the I
prospective issue of "rights" by the Jersey
Central. What are termed the "Flower" 'â 
stocks, including Brooklyn Rapid Transit,
People's Gas, New York Air Brake and Fed
eral Steel, were the objects of renewed bull- i
ish manipulation, and Sugar Refining, though ;
inactive, displayed a great deal of strength
1 Anaconda mining shares were a feature, ad- ':
vancing from 52 to 65% on the large buying
both here and in London, supposed to come
from interests â  which are arranging the new
copper mine combination. Otherwise London
was not a factor in our market, though foreign
interests were buyers of . Louisville and Nash
ville, Southern Railway preferred and Nor
i folk and Western, selling at the same time !
! high-grade bonds at the advances In that part 1
of the market. The sharp advance in the price â 
. of silver caused not a little notice to be paid
I to the securities of the Mexican roads and
: Mexican Central stock and bonds were con
spicuously strong under a good buying de- !
mand. ' . .
BRADSTREET'S ON TRADE CONDITIONS.
NEW YORK, April 28.â Bradstreet's to-mor
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, SATURDAY, APRIL 29, 1899.
row will say: Favorable weather conditions
find reflection in reoorts of good retail distri
bution of spring and summer- goods and in fair
rllllng-in orders from jobbers. Demand from
first hpnds for general merchandise Is If any
thing quieter, in keeping with the "between
season" period now at hand. Industrial ac
tivity continues specially marked, a pleasing
feature this week being the practical absence
of the unrest, particularly In the building
trades, noted for many years past about May 1.
While favoring retail distribution, the spring
like weather conditions, ' however, have been
the reverse of stimulaton as regards quota
tions of two of the country's greatest staples,
wheat and cotton. In these and in hog prod
ucts the tendency of values has been toward
a lower ranee. Winter wheat crop advices
have continued regularly unfavorable, hut good
weather, enabling a large area to be planted in
spring wheat, coupled with rather less active
export demand and denials of damage to Euro
pean crops, have weakened quotations of wheat
and flour at the leading Eastern and interior
markets.
New demands have been notably quiet in iron
and steel this week, the most notable feature
being large sales of Southern gray forge iron
at Pittsburs. Prices exhibit all their
old strength, and here the margin of profit on
finished products is undoubtedly very good.
The strength of the lumber market shows lit
tle Impairment. Wool as a whole is quiet and
steady, but a relatively higher range of values
abroad is indicated by. continued sales of Aus
tralian bonded wool to be re-exported. Copper
la notable among: the products advancing this
week, aided therein by large consumptive de
mand, but chiefly by talk of the great con
solidation of copper producing companies an
nounced toward the close of the week. Leather
is firmly held, strength being imparted to this
Hnd finished products by the small stocks or
hides available.
Partially compensating for the falling off in
exports of natural products, particularly ce
reals, Is the immense expansion in exports of
manufactured goods. . Shipments of such goods
in March ranged over $36,000,000, or 35 per cent
of all our exports, a proportion rarely If ever
approached before, and in view of probably
smaller takings of our natural products, fore
shadowing even more marked expansion in the
future, providing that prices do not advance
to a point prohibitive of free shipments.
Wheat, including flour, shipments for the
week aggregated 3.025.250 bushels, against 2,
--932,959 bushels last week, 4,449,009 bushels In the
corresponding week of 1898, 1,156.866 bushels In
1897, 1,260,449 bushels in 1896, .and 2,555,660 bush
els in 1895.
Since July 1 this season the exports of wheat
aggregate 96,290,281 bushels, against 195,592.695
bushels last year.
Business failures for the week number 184, as
compared with 187 last week. 245 in this week a
year ago, 244 in 1896, and 206 In 1895.
Canadian failures number 27, against SO last
week 22 in this week a year ago, 31 In 1597,
26 in 1896. and 36 in 1896,
DUN'S WEEKLY REVIEW OF TRADE.
NEW YORK, April 28.â R. G. Dun & Co.'S
Weekly Review of Trade will say:
All elements considered, it is rather sur
prising that business has not been set back
a little. The uncertainty about Samoa and
the prolonging of the difficulty in the Philip
pines might have counted for something, as
have the rising imports and falling exports,
the rise in foreign exchange, by some attributed â 
to foreign sales of copper stocks, and the hin
drance of manufacturing orders owing to the |
recent advance in prices.
But there has been full faith that foreign ;
difficulties would soon be cleared away, and j
the movement in exchange is generally con
nected with speculative rather than legitimate
operation; since accounts of trade still Indi- ;
cate a large excess of exports. After every ,
rise In prices manufacturers have to consider
whether hindered consumption with increased
production will make them difficult, but con
fidence with which combinations are formed
and extended still shows prevailing belief that
no danger point is near. â 
The state of foreign trade justifies confidence
rather than apprehension. It is the season
for th.- lowest export of staples and their
prices this year are extremely low, but the I
unprecedented foreign demand for manufac
tured products BO far compensates them. Ex
cess of imports in March was heavy and ap
parently hits been Inappropriate. The pres
ence of English merchants in Boston trying
to Bell English medium wool which would be
delivered there at about 32 cents, at the same
time that English buyers were taking a lit
tle fine territory for export, and German buy
ers of 300,000 pounds more Australian from bond,
shows the opposite trend of popular taste in
different countries. Clay worsteds here ad
vanced 7'-e. and serges sc, which strengthened
the tone of the wool market. The wool is,
relatively cheap "here and dear abroad, but |
concessions make the lowest prices of the year j
in all kinds, as heavy stocks carried for years
come into the presence of new wool, said to be
3(10.000,000 pounds. Manufacturers are not now
buying largely, though in four weeks 30,918,785 I
pounds were sold, of which 23,315,085 were
domestic, against 41,715,650 in the same weeks
two years ago, when wool was rising. Besse
mer pig is unchanged at Pittsburgh and gray
forge stro.-.g at $14 50, though 16,000 tons South
ern have been sold .iere at $14 20. There are
some large structural orders â a Philadelphia
building, for 17,000 tons, a Western bridge for
7000 tons, a Duluth elevator and the St. Law
rence bridge at Quebec though the general
run of orders in this line is light and no
large sales are reported, though Inquiries for j
000 tons are said to be in this market. Ad
vanced prices are noted in pipe ami decrease
in merchant orders, with small business in
and at the West in bars pending the
completion of the combination. But the gen- 1
oral decrease in new demand raises the ques- |
tion whether prices will be maintained after
current orders run out
Failures tor the week were IK4 in the United I
States", against Lfi2 last year; and 23 in Can- I
ada, against 16 last '-ear.

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
CHICAGO, April 23. Wheat was easy at the
opening owing to a large increase in Argen
tine shipments and lower cables, The weak
ness was only momentary, however, and the
market turned strong on complaints of, drought I
from Kansas and delayed seeding in the
Northwest on account of snow. May opened
Vie lower at 70%@71c, . rose to 71V4c, when
realizing checked the advance, and then weak
ened. May slipping off to 70%<Â§70%c. Private
and official crop reports were bad and out
side buying fairly heavy. This started an
upturn which was vigorously contested for
a while, but finally shorts ran to cover and
May advanced to 71% c, closing at 71% c. July
opened %c lower at 71%@72c, advanced to
72^c, reacted to 71% c, then recovered to 72c,
and closed at 72^c.
The corn market was narrow, but steady.

vanced to 34c, and declined to 38%0 at the
close.

and a- poor cash demand weakened uatÂ«<. May

NORTHERN WHEAT MARKET.
OREGON.
PORTLAND. April 28.â There was the usual
nervousness displayed In the Eastern markets
to-day, but the net benefit as far as the local
market was concerned amounted to nothing.
There is no tonnage offering and exporters are
unable to determine what the proper freight
rate on a cargo would be, and are accordingly
Indifferent about taking a plunge in the dark
by offering more for wheat' than can be got
out of it for milling purposes. Walla Walla
is still quoted at 59c, valley at 60c and blue
stem 61@62c.
Foreign wheat shipments from Portland for

weaker yesterday. Mexican dollars are still

DECIDUOUS AND CITRUS FRUITS.
Receipts were 416 boxes Cherries and 313
chests Strawberries.
There was a large shipment of 93 boxes of
Gooseberries in from Napa, and the first sales
were made at 50c per box, the price subse
quently declining to 35c, and some were left on
hand even then.
Cherries kept up under the usual light re
ceipts. There will be no Cherry glut this year
from present Indications. The first Royal Annes
of the season came In from Vacaville, and sold
at $1 50 per box.
More Blackberries came in from Covlna, sell
ing at 15c per basket. There was not much
change in Strawberries.
Oranges are in much lighter supply, but the
market lacks snap.

In sacks.
Harrison's circular says: ."During the week
there have been four .arrivals of coal from
Washington, with 12,550 tons; four from British
Columbia, 12,772 tons; three from Newcastle,
N. S. W.. 7952 tons; total, 33,274 tons. Under
ordinary circumstances the arrivals this week
would cause considerable of same to be yarded,
but the liberal deliveries to the Government
transports will leave but a small quantity to
go Into yard. Outside of the navy contracts
there is no change in the coal market, prices
are stationary and deliveries from yard are
about as usual, with the exceptions of two days
this week, which were showery, and caused
an Increased demand for household fuel. There
Is an increased inquiry for tonnage for coal
carriage from the coast colliers and better rates
are being offered. This Is caused by a scarcity
of local ships which were formerly engaged in
this trade, but have been diverted Into lumber
carrying principally to Australia, for which
generous rates have been paid. These will all
have arrived back with coal cargoes before the
end of the year. Our dealers have been some
what misled anticipating a softening of coal
freights from Australia and Great Britain, as
rates this week are quoted higher than last.
It was anticipated that grain shippers would be
chartering freely for the exportation of our in
coming grain at full figures, whereas the pres
ent Liverpool market offers them but little In
ducements to do so."

THE STOCK MARKET.
Business in local securities continued very !
active on the morning session of the Bond Ex- 1
change, and a wide list of stocks was dealt in.
Oceanic Steamship advanced to $S3, Hawaiian
Commercial to $111. Paauhau Plantation to
$43 25 and Gas and Electric to $70. Hutchtnson
fell off to $35 60.
On the afternoon call Hawaiian Commercial
advanced to $115. Paauhau declined to $42 25,
Contra Costa Water to $68 12% and Giant Pow
der to $71 50.
There was rather more tone to mining stocks,
but changes were insignificant. The telegram
from the pump said: "Elevator working stead
ily. Water In the 1750-foot level winze la 33
feet 8 inches below the station."
The delinquent assessment sale of the Occi
dental Consolidated Mining Company has been
postponed to May 2.
The official report from the Standard Con
solidated mine of Bodle District for the week
ending April 22 states that raise 3. in No. 10
vein on the 31S level, shows 10 inches good ore
in ! the top. There is no change in the east
crosscuts on the same level. The south drift
on the 380-foot level has a 16-inch vein, value
not stated, on the hanging wall side, and a.
vein 6 Inches wide on the footwall side, value
also not given. The north drift on the 336 level
has nearly connected with the chute. The stopes
on the 150 and 31S-foot levels in the Moyle, New
and No. 19 veins continue to yield fair grade
ore. The stope south of winze No. 1 in the
Maguire vein on the 528-foot level is yielding
good re. Some good ore is coming from the
Fortuna vein on the 582-foot level. In No. 8
south stop* on the 336-foot level in the C. and
D. ledge the assays are very high. No. 2 north
stope on the 470-foot level shows improvement.
During the week the mill crushed 213 tons of
ore. Average assay vanner tailings, $10 32.
Concentrates produced, 1.7 tons. Assay value.

the early morning tides are given In the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day In the order of occurrence as to time. The.
second time column gives the second tide of
the day, the third time column the third tidÂ»
und the' last or right hand column gives the

given are additions to the soundings on tha
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â ) precedes the height,
and then the number given is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference Is the mean of the lower low water*.

UNIVERSITY CADETS
IN A SHAM BATTLE
BERKELEY CAMPUS IS THE
SCENE OF MIMIC WAR.
BERKELEY, April 25.-Mnjor Edward
Field conducted the annual Inspection of
the University Corps of Cadets upon the
Berkeley campus to-day. The exercises
lasted through morning 1 and afternoon,
and were witnessed by a large number of
visitors. .
The troops assembled on the parade
ground shortly before 10 a. m., marching
in regiment formation to the lower cam
pus. Here they prepared for Inspection,
undergoing a most thorough examination .
of arms and uniforms at the hands if the j
inspecting officer. After two hours of
close inspection. Major Field pronounced
the condition of the department to be
thoroughly excellent, and spoke in high
terras of the appearance and bearing of
Previous to the sham battle, held in
the afternoon, the regiment was formed
for parade and review in honor of the
visiting ollicer. After exercises in bat- j
tallon movements under Major "William I
Ede ami Major W. Hackley, each com- I
nany was called out to contest for the
banner in competitive company drill. '
Company H of the second battalion, com
manded by Captain Oliver w. Dibble,
proved itself the most efficient and was
awarded the much coveted right of car- I
rying the colors next year.
Withdrawing for a while, the troops '
ranged themselves for the sham engage- j
ment. which proved the most interesting
oxercise of the day. Plenty of ammunl- !
tlon was provided to make the battle re
alistic. The defense took a position
Btretching across the center of the cam
pus, from which they awaited an attack
by a battalion of live companies from
below.
The attacking party, under Major Ede,
sent forward half its force in battalion
formation to dislodge the enemy, keeping
the remainder in reserve. In face of their
advance the defense withdrew tempora
rily to a strongly .... position in their
rear, rallying here in sufficient strength
to repel the advance. The attacking com
panies then retreated until the reserve
came up, two platoons from Company A
having meanwhile taken posit 1 by the
trees on the flank of the defense. From
here they pourod shot at the defense in
order to cover the retreat. As soon as
the reserve lent its support the whole at
tacking force advanced by rushes up the
hill, firing round after round. They com
peted the assault by a grand charge
which routed tho Demy, forcing it to
surrender.
Major Field, as soon as the battle was
over, made a sbort address to the troops
in which he complimented their work.
The exercises closed by the ceremony of
pn luting the flag, the band playing and
the troop.s presenting arms as the colors
â were dipped.
As the result of the year's work Pro
fr-FHor Frank Soule, commandant of the
corps, has sent the names of the follow
ing commissioned officers to Sacramento
to be enrolled as commissioned officers of
the same rank In the State militia:
To be colonel. Henry Walter Gibbons;
to hÂ« lieutenant colonel] William Ede; to
bo ma.iorsâ William A. Haekleyi* "William
H. Houston.
To be captainsâ William T. Mooney, i

Roy Fryer.

NO SOLACE FOR JURORS.
Judge Quinn Says They Were Either
Corrupt or Imbeciles.
OAKLAND. April 25. --"Crooked and
corrupt" rings in the ears of five jurors
who sat in Judge Quinn's court yester
day, and the sound does not please them.
William P. Courtney, one of these jur
ors, visited Judge Qulnn this afternoon
and sought a modulation of the expres
sion. ...
Judge Quinn was in no humor to modify
his language. "Get out of here," he said.
â¢I don't wish to see you around , this
court, i said you were crooked and cor
rupt and 1 meant it. I will stand by what
1 have said." _ , L. v .
In discussing the matter Judge Quinn
said the verdict in the Alice Moss case
was so thoroughly at variance with the
evidence and the court's instructions that
: if the jurors were not crooked and corrupt
! the" were absolutely devoid of intelli
ren'ce He said there were no middle
grounds If the jurors think themselves
aggrieved. Judge yuinn says, the law pro-
I vi<?es ample remedy for their alleged
i grievances.
A STRETCH OF TRACK
SINKS OUT OF SIGHT
' WRECK ON THE NARROW-GAUGE
BARELY AVERTED.
Alameda Office San Francisco Call,
142S Park street, April 28. .
The Santa Cruz train on the narrow- ;
gauge line, <lue at 10:50 this morning, '
barely escaped being wrecked at the
â¢â¢sink," about a quarter of a mile south
of the Pirn Leandro bridge, on the Bay
1 Farm Island marshes. The sink has been
a source of annoyance to the railroad
ever since the arrow-gauge line was put
in operation. It is a stretch of roadbed
about a hundred yards in length and ap
parently lilt on mud that covers a bot
tomless pit. A fortune h;is been expend
ed In lilling the place without producing
any substantial result. Several wrecks
have occurred at the place, and on one
occasion a fireman was pinned under his j
engine and drowned by the incoming tide. ',
Two trains passed over the road this ;
morning- but a short time ahead of th'i
Santa Cruz .special. The engineer of the
Santa Cruz train was keeping a sharp
lookout and discovered that the roadbed
had sunk almost out of sight. He ap
plied the air, bringing the train to a
stop just as the' forward trucks of the
locomotive reached the treacherous spot.
One of the train hands was sent to
Alameda. and a local train was backed
down the line to bring the passengers
into town. No through trains were run
over the road until late this evening.
HEALTH BOARD NAMED.
Health Officer Dunn Will Probably
Be Retained in Office.
OAKLAND, April 28.â Mayor Snow has
announced his Intention of appointing Its.
J. B. Wood, A. L. Cunningham and J.
Maher to the vacancies that will occur on
the Board of Health on May l. Dr. Wood
Is from Golden Gate in the annexed dis
trict. Dr. Cunningham is the only repre
sentative of the board who is not of the
allopathic school of medicine.
â¢ The holdover members are Drs. Kitch
ings and Hamlin. it is believed that the
ojd appointees of the board will not be
disturbed. All are Republicans and all
have proved trustworthy. The patronage
in the gift of the Health Board consists of
Health Officer, secretary, bacteriologist,
chemist, sanitary inspector, two assistant
inspectors, and city food Inspector and
veterinarian.

Oakland Ofllce 3an Francisco Call,
90S Broadway. April 28.
The remarkable rise in the value of
Contra Costa water stock has Bet the
people of this city to wondering. The fig
ures in the accompanying table show bet
ter than anything else could do the re
markable effects of tights in the Council
over water rates. The rapid rise in the
value of the stock at this time is a per
fect enigma, for unless there be some
facts unknown to the public the income
of the company after the 30th of June will
be less than It is at this time.
On July 1 a new ordinance will go into
effect, which is said to be considerably
lower than the ordinance adopted by the
"Solid Six" last year, and which caused
several riots. Yet the value of the stock
has increased nearly 50 per cent since the
last day of last'year, and why this should
be with the prospect of a decreased In
come is hard to understand.
On the 9th of next-month, according to
President "Watkinson of the consolidated
companies, the papers will be (lied annex
ing the Oakland Water Company to the
old corporation, and at the same time
Messrs May ward and Dingee will receive
their one and a half millions in stock. At
the present valuation this represents a
value of $1072,500, upon which, according
to the State law. "reasonable interest"
must be paid. As neither company has
paid any interest for several years, it Is
hard to understand, with the new.ordin
ance going Into effect, how such an im
mense capitalization can be made to pro
duce an income for its holders. Yet that
such an income is expected is very evi
dent from the rapid rise in the market
value of the stock. ~Vr-
The Contra Costa Company has discov
ered that it has a claim against the city
of Oakland for $127,000.. This amount is
being carried on tts books and Is for water

CALIFORNIA'S MINERALS.
Last Year's Record of Production
Shows an Increase.
The State Mining Bureau has issued the
record of California's mineral product for
1898, as compiled by its statistician,
Charles G. Yale. It shows a gratifying
increase in the production of mineral
wealth.
During IS9B thirty-seven mineral sub
stances were commercially produced on
a scale worthy of record. The valuation
of the total mineral product is $27,289,079.
In 1897 it was $25,142,441, In 1896 $24.28-1,398,
In 1896 $22,844,664.
The gold product Is placed at $15,90*.4 .?.
a slight increase over the $15,871,401 in 1897.
As the drought of last year lessened the
product by some millions this demon
strates an active development of gold
mining.
Copper takes second place for the first
time with a product of 21,543,229 pounds,
valued at $2,475,168. a very great Increase.
Petroleum increased to 2,249,088 bar
rels, valued at $2,376,420. Quicksil
ver, so long second in importance,
holds third place. The product increased
to 31,092 flasks, worth $1,188,626. Other Im
portant products follow: Asphalt, $482
--175: silver, $414,055; rubble, $445,395; brick,
$571,362; coal, $337,475.
The banner county for all mineral prod
ucts was Shasta, with $3,510,723 to its
credit. Other ranks are: Nevada, $2,072,
--604; Amador, $1,849,846; Tuolumne, $1,757.
--735; Los Angeles, $1,732,357, etc. While
Nevada leads in gold, Shasta's additional
copper puts it ahead.

CYCLERS' ROAD RACES.
Training for the Big Hundred Mile
Relay Race May 14.
The annual relay race of the Associated
Wheelmen will take place this year on
Bunday, -May 14, and five prominent clubs
â earns In training for the event.
They are the Olympic, Bay City, Garden
City, Reliance and Acme wheelmen. There
i.s also a possibility that the California.
Encinal and Imperial clubs will enter :
teams.
In <â  msequence of the near approach of
the race the clubs that have decided to j
entei will all hold road races to-morrow
over the Sun Leandro-Haywards course.
The Olympics will have a ten-mile try
out around the triangle; tii>- Hay Cltya a
Jive-mile unpaced event'from San Leandro
to Haywards, straight away. The Acme
and Reliance clube will both have ten
mile try-outs, and the Garden Cltya will
train their men over the East San Jose
course. All of these events will tak>: place
to-morrow forenoon, the San Francisco
contingent going over on the 9 a. m. broad
gauge <
No correct idea of the formation of the
teams can he had until a few days before
the race itself. Any one familiar with the
sport and the racing men can guess at
seven or eight men who will undoubtedly
ride on each team on account of their
known ability, but the two odd ones will
me almost impossible to decide upon even
with the club managers themselves until
the last momi nt.
Chairman Loop of the racing board.
accompanied by J. H. Xa?h, will ride
around the l(K>-mile relay course to-mor
| row to Bee thai everything is in order.

Anxious Mrs. Jones.
The following letter was received by the
Coroner yesterday:
SAN FRANCISCO. April 27, 18M.
Coroner Hill â Dear Sir: I am very much |
wrought up over the disappearance of my I
daughter, Mrs. Tom Bozzle. She has been |
away from home for two days and I fear she j
has committed suicide. Please do all in your
power to help me find her, dead or alive. Her
anxious mother, MRS. ANNIE JONES.
It Is believed to be a faked letter, writ
ten by some one whose idea of humor is :
the giving of trouble to others. The Cor
oner has done nothing: to find the one who
wrote the letter, and as there was no ad
dress given no more attention will be paid
to it. %fr-Jm

A GRAND PICNIC.
Congregation of St. Dominic's Church
Will Hold Its Reunion at Mira
bel Park Next Saturday.
The second annual nicnic, excursion and
reunion of the congregation of St. Dom
inic's Church will be held Saturday, May
6, at Mirabel Park. Extensive arrange
ments have been made by those In charge
of the affair and it is ex"pected that fully
, two thousand poople will go. The excur-
I sinnists will leave the Tiburon ferry at 9
I o'clock in the morning. E. J. Dollard.
chairman of the committee of arrange
! ments, declares that no expense has been
: spared and nothing forgotten that can
i add to the pleasure of those who will at
! tend. There will be tame! and dancing
and an excellent band has been engaged
to furnish the music. The gate priaes
that are to be given 'iway are said t<> he
the handsomest that have ever
awarded at any church picnic that has
' been given in this city. The men of the
' eongrpc-ation constitute a committee of j
! the whole, who are determined that the
I grand reunion shall be a gala day in the ;
! history of St. Dominic's ChurcH.

Another FhnfTio and cut of the leaâtie
cards during the week as resulted in
an entirely new hand In the game of per
centage. Santa Cruz, by taking the Oak
lnne! series, has drawn away from the
bunch and is now comfortably In the
.end. with San Francisco ant! Wntsonvllle
Eightlng for second place. San Jose is all [
by itself, having broken even on the num- i
her of games won and lost, while Oak
land and Sacramento are doins a double
Ptunt in the last notch. The taJlenders,
however, are so well up that the finish
of the present week's series is likely to
find one or the other up among the lead
ers, if not actually in first place The
crank who would undertake to name the
leader a month from now must be gifted
with supernatural powers. The games
won and lost and the percentages are as
follows:

Manager Harris has made a great find
and Pop Swett will not get back Into the
game. Pop found it too great a task to
pet into .-thing like his old condition, bo
he gave it up. Last week Harris was in a
quandary; having no catcher, so in des
peration he signed young Charles Swin
dell of the Stanford University team for
the Sacramento series. The youngster
made an excellent showing and Harris
at once cured him for the season. In
addition to being a. hard hitter Swindell is
an exceptionally strong and accurate '
thrower to bases, and above ail he is a
fast man on the paths. Hitherto this ha*
been a decided weakness in the team, but
Hank thinks Swindell will help the entire i
team in that line.
The new Oakland grounds at Golden
Gate will be formally opened next Sun-
Say morning by the Oaklands and Sacra
mentos in a regular league test. Ewing
has secured one of the prettiest sites in
the State for a ball park. The entire in-
I'losure is as level as a floor and the out
tield Is covered with .1 luxuriant crop of
grass, which, however, is kept closely
cut. This week he is having sawdust
worked Into the soil on the diamond and
by Sunday the grounds will be in perfect
condition.
Sacramento will present several changes
In Its make-up this week. Manager
Kripp has written to Harris that he has
linally secured Harvey and that he will
probably pitch one of the games against
Oakland. Krlpp has also secured a new
Inflf-lder, but has not made his name pub
lic yet.
Santa Cruz has one great advantage
over the other league teams in having
heavy hitting batteries. As a rule ball
teams do not rely much on the stick work
of the men in the points, but Donlin and :
Balsz are exceptionally heavy hitters,
while Pace can find the ball with any of
them. Daubenbis; the reserve pitcher, is
also a good sticker.
Krug of San Francisco continues to
land on the ball hard and often. Last
Sunday at Sacramento he put it up
against the left field fence three times, j
Krug is making me errors at short, but '
he never thinks of his record, going for
everything In sight and covering more
ground and accepting more chances than
any other intielder in the league.
The Oak! cripples are rapidly round-
Ing into form again and ihe team is prac
ticing at Recreation Park every after
noon, while their own diamond is being
put into first-class condition. Donovan
has almost entirely recovered from his
Charley horse, while Billy Hammond's
hand Is about healed up.
Dunleavy, who sprained his ankle three
weeks ago, has recovered, and will regu
larly play left field in place of Borland,
who has been released. With Donovan at
center and Hardin at right, where he will
be stationed permanently; this will give
Oakland one of the best run-getting out
fields in the league, as well as a sure
fielding and accurate long-distace throw-
Ing trio.
Fred Swanton tells a funny story of
how Donlin got into the. game. The big
pitcher was formerly a foot-racer and
â¢with a companion started south to
"work" the orange-growers. While in
Los Angeles the ball team there had some
hard luck and somebody asked Donlin if
he could play, as the â  â¢ atn had a game on
for that day and nobody to fill up the
gap. Donlin said he could play all right,
though In truth he nad never played a
game In his life. However, he went into
right field and managed to accept the
only chance offered, an easy fly. The op
posing team had a "dub" In the box and
Donlin fairly slaughtered him every time
he went to bat. Two days later they got
Donlin into another game and th( Los
Angeles pitcher was disabled almost at
the start. The captain asked Donlin if
he could pitch and again received an af
firmative reply. Donlin was hard as a
rock and although he had never pitched
a ball before he had terrific speed and
nobody could touch him. During the game
he noticed thai some of the halls he threw
â would start In a line and suddenly shoot
off to one side. From that time he began
to practice pitching and now his curves
are simply marvelous. And he is only a
novice yet.

PROPOSALS
To Print, Publish and Distribute the De-
linquent Tax List for IS9S.
OFFICE OF THE CLERK OF THE i
Board of Supervisors of the City and
County of San Francisco, April 25, l*:>'.'.
in accordance with Resolution No. 2026
(Fourth Series) of the Board of Supervis-
ors, SEALED PROPOSALS will be re-
ceived In open session of the Board of Su-
pervisors on MONDAY AFTERNOON
May 8, 1899, from 2:30 to 3 o'clock, for the
printing, publishing and distributing of
the delinquent tax list for the year 1898,
to contain the names of the persons and
a description of the property delinquent
and the amount of taxes and costs due
opposite each name, and a notice unless
the taxes delinquent, together with the
costs and percentage, are paid, the prop-
erty upon which said taxes are a lien
will be sold at public auction. Such
publication to be made and thoroughly
distributed on a business day, at least
once in each week, for three successive
weeks, in a pamphlet form as a supple-
ment to a daily newspaper of general cir-
culation, published in the city and
county of San Francisco, in the
English language, and said supple-
ment must be Pent and distrib-
uted on the days specified with every
number of the paper issued and distrib-
uted, by carriers, by mail or otherwise
The first issued to be so distributed on or
before the first day of June. 1399 and the
others weekly thereafter, and to desig-
nate the time and place of sale and in all
respects conform io the provisions of the !
Political Code and to be done under the >
direction and to the satisfaction of Ed-
ward I. Sheehan, Tax Collector of said !
City and County; also, to leave at the Tax
Collector's office not lees than one thou- I
sand (1000) copies of each issue before any j
demand on the Treasury therefor will I
be approved. Within one week after the
first publication of said list fifty (50)
copies thereof, printed upon one side only
of a quality of paper which can be written
upon with ink, to be satisfactory to said
Tax Collector, to be delivered at his office
for use in the office. Also fifty (50) cloth-
bound copies, interleaved, to be delivered
to the Tax Collector's office within two
weeks after the first publication.
The Tax Collector must have the privi-
lege at any and all times during the exe-
cution of the said contract of witnessing
the same ami giving instructions relative
thereto, either personally or through his '
chief deputy, or any deputy he may desig-
nate.
All proofs must be read and submitted
for correction as follows:
Firstâ Proofs must be hrst read by copy
and corrected by the printer.
Secondâ Submitted In slips to the Tax
Collector for correction.
Thirdâ Revise to be submitted to and
passed upon by the Tax Collector, or his
chief deputy, or deputy; if corrections or
alterations are made by said Tax Collec-
tor, or his chief deputy, or deputy, a cor-
rected revise must be presented to and i
approved by him -prior to the form being !
struck off, and as the work progresses
when a form is struck off a copy of the
same to be filed with said Tax Collector.
Payment to be made as hereinafter pro-
vided, and the bill presented to be veri-
fied by the affidavit of a proper and re-
sponsible person acceptable to the Com-
mittee on Printing and Salaries of the
Board of Supervisors, said affidavit to be
made before a notary public in and for
the said City and County, and to set
forth that the contract for printing, pub-
lishing and distributing the Delinquent
Tax List for the year 1898, has been fully
and faithfully executed and accomplished
in strict conformity to and with these
specifications.
The contractor or contractors will re-
ceive a demand upon the Treasury of the
said City and County for the payment of
the contract price in United States gold
coin, when satisfactory evidence Is fur-
nished the said Committee on Printing
and Salaries that the said contract has
been fully and faithfully executed and ac-

PROPOSALS
For Burial of Indigent Dead.
OFFICE OF THE CLERK OF THE
Board of Supervisors of the City and
County of San Francisco. April 25, 1899.
In accordance with Resolution No. 2628
(Fourth Series) of the Board of Super-
visors, SEALED PROPOSALS will be re-
ceived in open session of the Board of
Supervisors on MONDAY AFTERNOON.
May 8, 1899, from 2:30 to 3 o'clock, for
the interment of such of the indigent
dead as are required to be buried at the
charge of the City and County of San
hrancisco. Including the City and County
Smallpox Hospital, for thp term of one
year, beginning July 1, 1899, and ending
June 30, 1900, in such cemetery or ceme-
teries in San Mateo County wherein
burials may be allowed.
NOTICE.â The coffins to be made of
tlirf-{'-f|uarter-inch surface redwood clear
lumber, to be lined with muslin and pil-
lowed inside, and to be shellacked and
varnished outside, and the tops to be
fastened down with not less han six
(screws. In all cases where persons have
died from smallpox or other contagious
diseases the coffin shall be lined with No
10 zinc instead of with muslin, and her-
metically sealed, and all coffins used
must be subject to the inspection and ap-
proval of the officer in charge of the in-
stitution from which the body of tne
decedent is to be buried, and must In all
eases be taken to such institution for the
purpoÃe of having the deceased placed
therein.
No hid will be considered from any par-
ty who has not been engaged In tlie un-
dertaking business in this City and Coun-
ty and conducted said business in a rep-
utable manner for a period of at least six
months immediately preceding the date
hereof.
The grave to be excavated to a depth
of at least six feet.
No body to be removed from the insti-
tution where it lies awaiting 1 burial with-
in a period of twenty-four hours after
death, except by express order of the
Superintendent or officer in charge of
said institution.
The contractor will be required in all
cases on the death of the indigent person
from infectious disease to inter the body
of said decedent within such time as may-
be designated by notice from the Superin-
tendent or officer in charge of the institu-
tion where the death may have occurred,
and in all other cases within thirty-six
hours after notification by the proper of-
ficer.
The contractor will further be required
to convey the bodies of decedents com-
mitted to his charge- for interment direct
from the institution from which he re-
ceives the same to the cemetery, and then
and there bury the same. And will not
be permitted upon any consideration to
convey such bodies to any intermediate
reetink-place, or to keep the same in his
custody for a longer period than that re-
quired for their transit to the cemetery.
Should the friends of any decedent "ap-
ply at >the institution where such deced-
ent may be lying awaiting burial, for per-
mission to bury the same, and prove. to
the Superintendent or officer in charge of
said institution that their purpose Is to
provide a decent burial, and that they
have no intention and will not allow the
body to be used for the purpose of dissec-
tion, then and in that case the body of
s.ilrl deceased shall be delivered to "said
friends, who shall take charge thereof
and make the necessary arrangements for
the funeral with any undertaker they
may think fit: but in such event the con-
tractor aforesaid shall have no claim
against the city for the burial of said de-
cedent.
The party to whom the contract may
be awarded shall be required, in all cases
to procure from the Superintendent of the
cemetery wherein the burials have bet'n
made a certificate of the proper burial of
each person under the provisions of his
contract and of these specifications; and
such certificate must, in all cases, be at-
tached to the demand of said contractor
when presented to the Board of Super-
visors for approval, and must state the
names of the decedents and the institu-
tions from which the body was procured.
In case of the violation of any of the
above conditions, the contract shall be
terminated by said City and, County.
The party to whom this contract is
awarded will be required, prior to or at
the time of the execution of said contract
to pay the cost of advertising this noti< a
in three daily newspapers, and to also
pay the cost of the publication in the of-
flclal newspaper of the Resolution award-
ing him the contract.
All interments of indigent persons the
expenses of which must be borne by the
City and County (other than those from
any of the public institutions 1 ) must be
authorized by the Health Officer issuing
a permit therefor, and all bills for such
interments to be approved by the Health
Officer prior to being presented to this
Hoard. All demands for the burial of in-
digent persons from any of the public in-
stitutions to be approved by the Superin-
tendent of the institution at which the
death occurs, prior to being presented to
this Board.
Bidders will estimate and state a speci-
fic amount for each interment.
In order to preserve the uniformity and
to facilitate the award, the Board has
resoived to receive no bids unless made

($5000) dollars, required within six days af

case, the paid sum shall be collected and

Governor Gag-e's Proposition So
Dubbed by the Labor Council.
The Pan Francisco Labor Council at
Its mooting last night unanimously adopt
ed the following resolution, which was
presented by Delegate P. H. McCarthy:
Wherpap, It has came to the notice of the
Labor Council that gambling rlens have been
opÂ»nly conducted in the most popular districts
of the city, an.l that the same have existed
without any apparent molestation by the po
lice; and

nicious oorruj'ters of our youth, and further
urge that the Chip' of Police he rerjupsted to
continue the crusade until the gamble are
forced to quit their evil traffic, co that this
diefracp be eliminated from our shopping <iis
trlcts.
Having disposed of the gambling evil
the Council n<xt paid its attention to Gov
ernor Gage for his action regarding the
appointment of a Labor Commissioner. It
â wan stated that the Governor desired to
have the Council submit five names to
him from which he might select a Com
missioner. This was characterized ad
giving the labor element a. gold brick. It
was only a big bluff from which he could
decline to appoint any one of the names
submitted to him and which would give
him an opportunity to appoint some
heeler from the southern portion of the
State.
The Council Indignantly declined to

dress of the secretary, and can communi
cate with him as he lid when he was a
candidate last year and at the time he
was looking for the labor vote.

cott against Siebe & Green's bill posting

make concessions with the union bill
posters.

THE NEW STATE
NORMAL SCHOOL
AWAKES TO LIFE
Governor Gage Names
Trustees.
HUSTLING TO BE PRINCIPAL
THE CITY'S INSTITUTION" WILL
BE DIFFERENT.
Judge M. Cooney a Trustee â A High
Standard, a Two Years'
Course and Bright
Future.
The new State Normal School at San
Francisco, which the Legislature created |
and provided with $20,000 for two years, j
will be an entity In a day or two, when |
Its five trustees who have been appointed j
by Governor Gage are publicly announced, j
This new institution will start out in a !
small way, but it will mean much to the
educational interests of the State and |
city and promises to soon rise to great '
importance.
The appointement of the trustees and j
the organization that must be completed !
within sixty days have been the subjects
of much quiet work, worry and specula
tion In some quarters. It is known that
Judge M. Cooney, who is mainly respon
sible for the success in establishing the
school, Is one of the trustees named. Sev
eral well known gentlemen have sought
the honor. Though the appointments are
made the public announcement awaits
the Governor's pleasure.
There are three leading candidates for
the office of principal, or rather "presi
dent," as the heads of the State normal i
schools are now officially termed. Laura j
D. Fowler, who has bo long been principal
cf the city normal school, which the State
Institution supplants, would like the place
and may get it. Madison Babcock and \V
D. Kingsbury of the San Francisco
School department are hot after the
plum.
Within the limits of the general laws
regulating State normal schools the Board I
of Trustees, of which the Governor and !
Superintendent of Public Instruction are
ex-offlcio members, has quite absolute au
thority in organizing and running the
school. They may fix the length of the
course, the entrance requirements, etc.,
about as they please, regulate the number
of teachers, the salaries and so on.
It is known that what is to be done has
been pretty certainly outlined. The trus- |
tees, with their $10,000 a year, will try to
take up the city institution when it 'will
be abandoned at the end of this school
year, enlarge and improve it somewhat
and begin an evolution toward a great
institution. The Board of Education will
be asked to turn over the present prem
ises and equipment, and if it does so the i
new State Normal School will be opened |
in the old quarters on Powell street, near
Sacramento. The present Normal School,
which is yet a municipal institution,
though the expenses are paid by the
students, has 125 pupils and two teachers. I
Probably the new school will open with
two teachers. Improvements will be made
and much equipment added. If other
quarters must be had they will be rented
somewhere.
It is understood that the new quarters
will differ quite radically from the other
State normal schools at San Jose ChiCO,
Los Angeles and San Diego. It will. if the
ideas of Mr. Cooney and others are fol
lowed, as they probably will be. At the
other State normal schools the course is
four years and grammar school graduates
are admitted. The first two years are
devoted to scientific and literary studies,
so that they are practically high schools.
Pedagogy figures in the last two years.
Judge Cooney believed that the institu
tion should be purely a normal school
devoted to the training of teachers. It is
proposed to make the standard of admis
sion a graduation from a high school or
its equivalent. School studies will be sim
ply reviewed and pedagogy and practical
experience in teaching will be the main
features of the course. With the high
Ftandard for admission th.> course will
be made two years only. It Is expected
that students may spend half their time
in the city school rooms, getting practical
experience. It is proposed to turn out
teachers only and a high grade of them.
The City Normal School has been prac
tically a State one. for a large percentage
of its students have gathered from vari
ous'counties In the otate and with its
one year's course its diplomas have been
recognized in over thirty counties. It is
expected that the high standard to be
adopted and the advantages of the city,
Including its schools, will draw a large
attendance from all over the State.
In looking ahead its sponsors see a suc
cess that in two years will bring about
the building of a big edifice, an attend
ance of hundreds and adequate appropria
tions.
It was all the quick result of the clos
ing of the City Normal School by the
retrenching new board and a mustering
of its friends for State support.

Brought on a Complete Syste
matic Collapse.
I >r. John Gallwejr, formerly health ofn
i . r. am] our- of the most popular and best
known physicians <>f this city, is lying
dangerously HI at his home, 1331 J^eaven
worth Btreet
])r. Gallwey's illness is, as the physi
i cinns who are attending him confidently
suit.- chiefly due to his strong devotion
to duty, ft has never mattered in the
I whole course of his professional life at
what hour the call came â If there was a
i sufferer needing attendance Dr. Gallwey
would always cheerfully leave his bed
and go to the relief of the ailing one.
Nighi after night he has gone almost
without rest simply because he deemed
ii his duty to lay self aside and go to the
of his patients. This strain finally
told upon his system. The symptoms of
a general break-down came gradually at
tirst, and the doctor tried to laugh them
down. Tired nature could not be over
ridden in this manner, and finally he was
forced to acknowledge himself sick and
take to his bed.
Ever since he has been attended by the
best physicians in the city, and they feel
quite confident of his recovery. 11 is hun
dreds of friends In and about San Fran
cisco are watching him almost as closely
.-is his devoted family, and on every hand
i hopes are expressed of a speedy recov
i <>ry-
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, SATURDAY, APRIL 29, 1899.
TOWN AND PEOPLE READY
TO HONOR DEWEY DAY
The Flag That Floated Over Dewey Will Be
Spread to the Breeze in Golden Gate Park.
T"*IREPARATIONS for the celebration of Dewey day on Monday are com-
PREPARATIONS sunrise till sunset the name and deeds of the hero of
plete, and from sunrise till sunset the name and deeds or the hero of
J[ Manila will be sung and honored throughout the city. i
In the celebration at Sunset Park by the Sons of "Vermont two men
who fought with the gallant admiralâ Carpenter O. H. Hilton of the Boston
and Gunner J. C Evans,' who were with Dewey on that history-making May
dayâ are expected to take part.
Judge Edward A. Belcher, president of the celebration committee, has
addressed the following letter to C. R. Ransom at Mare Island Navy-yard,
and It is expected his request "will be granted:
â  "*"C. R. RANSOM, Vallejo. Pal.â Sir: We shall be glad to have Carpenter O. H.
Hilton, sometime of the Boston, and Gunner J. C. Evans, sometime ofythe Boston,
both at the battle of Manila Bay, participate in the celebration of Dewey Day at Sun
pet Park. Will you please request them to report to me at the foot of Market street,
narrow gauge ferry, this city, on the morning of May 1, "Dewey Day," at about 8:30,
and I will furnish them transportation from San Francisco to the park and return to
this city, and invite them to a place on the stage during the ceremonies. Very sin
cerely yours. . â¢ EDWARD A. BELCHER, President.
The beautiful American flag presented to the Park Commissioners by the
sailors from the United States ship Raleigh will be thrown to the. breeze on
Monday. This flag has only been used once, and then in compliment to the
men who brought it here from Manila. It is singularly appropriate to dis
play it on Dewey day, coming as it did from the men who fought under
Admiral Dewey on that memorable Ist of May a year ago. It will be dis
played near the music stand. A special programme ft appropriate music will
be rendered by the Park Band -during the afternoon.
The day will be duly observed on the -water front. All the State offices
will be closed, and the wharfingers, collectors, carpenters and sweepers will be
given a holiday. Almost every ship in port will be decorated with bunting,
and the battleship lowa will fire an Admiral's salute.
The American ship captains are going to make it a point to have their
ships decorated, and as very few of them have crews aboard, the captains
are going to sen to It themselves that the bunting is spread. The British
ship captains propose to follow suit, and as every steamer, ferry-boat, yacht
and river steamer will be flying her colors, the bay should present a strik
ing appearance.
Some pleasant correspondence has passed between President Edward A.
Belcher of the celebration committee. Major General Shafter and Captain
Henry Glass, commandant of Naval Training Station. Ye.rba Buena Island,
regarding the firing of salutes on Monday. It has finally been arranged that
a salute of seventeen guns will be fired from the United States ship Pensa
cola at 8 a. m. on Monday. At noon the land batteries will belch forth their
salute of seventeen guns in honor of the occasion.
The employes of the California Northwestern Railroad Company will pic
nic at Mirabel Park on Dewey day.
The Central Methodist Episcopal Sunday-school, Mission street, between
Sixth and Seventh, will celebrate Dewey day by special exercises at the regu
lar Sunday-school session, 12:45 o'clock to-morrow. Special music has been
prepared by the choir. A patriotic address will be delivered by Rev. George
"C. Adams, D. D., pastor of the First Congregational Church. There will be
other exercises suitable to the occasion. The public is invited to be present.
A social entertainment and ball will be given by the Point Lobos Improve
ment Club at Richmond Hall on Dewey day. The following is the pro
gramme of the evening exercises:
Remarks by the chairman, D. 1... Fitzgerald; overture, Instrumental duet, James
Vincent, Miss Ella Fletcher; song, "The Quaker" (Stephen Adams), George T. Balch;
club swinging. Miss Lottie J. Graeber; song. "Waiting" (Mlllard), Miss Alice Vincent;
specialties. Master Fay Dougherty: song, tenor solo, John H. Desmond; reading, "Bil
ly's Pets," Fred L. Shaw; song. "Only a Dream" (C. Bohm), Miss Sydnia Barnard;
specialties. Miss Lottie Whitman; soprano solo. selected. Miss P. Denny; reading,
"Uncle Peter and the Trolley Car" (Neall), Miss Lottie J. Grabber; quartet. "Come
"Where My Love Lies Dreaming," Mrs. J. H. Plovers, Miss Elsie Smith, Miss Bertie
Smith, J. L. Marshall; cornet solo, I. C. Cogglns; patriotic song, "Dewey Day," writ
ten and composed for the occasion. Miss V. K. Xlppert.
The parishioners of St. Francis' Church, corner of Montgomery avenue
and Vallejo street, have determined to celebrate Dewey day with an annual
gathering and picnic at Shell Mound Park, Berkeley. As Monday will be a
legal holiday a large crowd is expected. The affair Is in charge of the
young men of the parish. A band will leave on the 9 o'clock boat. The boat
will leave the foot of Market street every half hour. The games will start
at 1:30 p. m.
IS GRATIFIED AT
SMITH'S PROMOTION
MAJOR GENERAL ANDERSON
ARRIVES FROM MANILA.
Duboce Was Given Too Much Credit
in the Engagement of February
sâ Major Sime Is Praised.
Ma.inr General T. M. Anderson. V. P. V.,
who ia also brigadier general in the regu
lar arm) . arrived from Manila on the
America Maru yesterday and Is a guest at
the Palace. General Anderson received or
ders to come to San i^rancisco, and after
telegraphing his arrival to the depart
ment at Washington he will await fur
ther instructions here. It is more than
likely that he will be assigned to one of
the four departments now vacant. These
are the Departments of the Lakes, Gulf,
I'latt<* and Columbia.
General Anderson first came to this city
from Alaskn. whore he was colonel
of the Fourteenth Infantry, and he was
placed in charge of the first Philippine
expedition which left this port soon after
the outbreak of the war, and included
the First California Volunteers. In an in
terview regarding various topics last
evening. General Anderson said:
"I was very much gratified at the pro
motion of Colonel James F. Smith to bp
brigadier general. I consider that hf>
should have had it long before he did. It
should have come after the battle of Ma
nila, where Smith distinguished himsHf.
He has proven himself a good soldier and
a prudent and able adviser. He has been
intrusted with a number of important ne
gotiations, notably the reconstruction of
Negros, and has performed the.n nil with
excellent judgment. His promotion is a
deserved one.
"Regarding the Filipinos, I must say
that they are natural born fighters, with
a good deal of military spirit. All things
considered, they got up a very good or
ganization; but, fortunately, though they
had better arms than we had. they did ;
not know how to use them. They cannot
use artillery effectively either. In thÂ».
battle of February 5 I captured seven
runa fn>m themâtwo Krupp and five old
fashioned muzzle-loaders.
"Agulnaldo is shrewd and unscrupulous.
He has the sympathy of the people, but
lately he has been but a mere figure-head,
because the revolutionary junta, com
posed of a set of professional revolution
ists, has taken the matter in its own
hands. The Malolos crowd determined to
achieve independence if they could and
then divide up the church property, which
is very valuable, abrogating all the old
Spanish concessions and appropriate them
to their own use.
"General Whf-aton's flying column is
performing effective work against the in
surgents. The object of the present cam
paign is to drive them from a railroad
from Manila to the northern part of the
island and force them to the hills.
"I must say that the First California is
an excellent regiment. Major Sime has
also proved himself a capable soldier. He
is quiet and unobtrusive and thoroughly
posted in military affairs. An injustice
has been done both to him and Smith
in ascribing most of the credit of the
engagement of February 5 to Duboce.
While the latter did good work, his com
mand was not as important as that of
Smith or Sime. The little that Duboce
h;ul to do h*- did very well. He was held
back in command of the reserve. Most
of it was drawn away, leaving him in
command of two companies. An attack
w;is made on them by a lot of Filipinos
from the village near by. Duboce drove
them out, but he did not take part in thr>
more severe and decisive part of the bat
tk-; mind you. through no fault .of his
own. To mention him and not the others,
however, appears to me to be an unin
tentional injustice."
BLOCKADE IN CHINATOWN.
Police Have Inaugurated a New Sys
tem Which Stops Gambling
Games.
The police und<=T Lieutenant Esnla and
Sergeant Donovan have established what
i? practically a blockade of the gambling
club? in Chinatown, and as a result all
the doors are wide open and the rooms
where once crowds of Mongolians held
their breath watching the fall of a bean
are vacant. â¢
The blockade was actually begun a few
days ago, and it has now become thor
ough and effective. The squad men and
uniformed police are working on a rotary
system. Two men will visit a club; about
five minutes later two more men will drop
I in, while the other two are at the far end
of the alley, and so it goes on until each
! man has visited each club or suspicious
1 place. By the time the last pair has com
| pleted the rounds the first pair is back
[again, and It Is thus almost impossible
to carry on any Â£nmfÂ». Formerly it was
customary to make spasmodic raids.
either by trickery or force, but these only
stopped certain games temporarily. I'n
der the now system it Is impossible for
any games to run. If the police find a half
dozen men assembled in any of the rooms
they promptly kick them into the streets.
Of cnurpe, under this system very few
arrests are made; but the police are really
doing more work than if they were ar
resting fifty or a hundred men nightly.
with no evidence that gambling was being
carried on.
The best evidence of the efficacy of the
plan comes from the Chinese themselves,
who are loud in their denunciations of It.
One of the club managers says: "They
come into place one hundred times in two
days. If they find any man here hut our
working man they kick him out in the
street. I do no business. I might just
as well close up. for then I would not
have to buy any light."
MANY ARTICLES SOLD
IN CHARITY'S CAUSE
THE C. L. A. S.'S BAZAAR AGAIN
THRONGED.
Archbishop Riordan Unable to At
tend Through â Features
for This Evening.
Illness prevented Archbishop Riordan
from attending the bazaar of the Catholic
Ladies' Aid Society last night. His Grace
sent his regrets to the ladies who are in
charge of the affair and promised to be
on hand some time during the week.
As was expected there was a slight fall
ing off in the attendance last night. Dur-
Ing the evening a large number of visitors
thronged the fair and bought the pretty
and useful articles on exhibiton or tried
their luck in the games that are in
progress.
Knicknacks. needle work and other ar
ticles are rapidly sold, as the prices asked
are less than cost. It is expected that
even a larger crowd than that which was
present on the opening night will bo on
hand this evening. An entertaining pro
gramme has been arranged expressly for
the occasion. Many organizations have
signified their intention of visiting the
bazaar before its close as the great work
the Catholic Ladies' Aid Society has been
doing in the cause of charity has won
their recognition.
WILL ARGUE TO-NIGHT.
Debating League of Northern Califor
nia Will Discuss the Czar's
Universal Peace.
The second semi-annual convention of
the Debating League nf Northern Cali
fornia will convene in business session at
9 o'clock this morning in the Girls' High
School auditorium for the purpose of or
ganization. This afternoon the election
of officers -will take place, and to-night
there will be a debate between the Stock
ton and Lowell high schools. The subject
is "That the peace proposal of the Czar
is practicable." For the affirmative are:
C A. Miller, N. Rutherford and C. A
Thorp, and for the negative Miss B Wil
son. F. A. Mandel and A. H. Weller. The
judges are Superintendent Webster, T. O.
Crawford and Attorney Goodsell. The
convention of the league was prefaced last
night by an interesting programme of a
social nature that was given in the audi
torium of the High School, at which Presi
dent W. N. Frickstad of the league pre
sided.
