FULL EXTENT OF
WIDBER'S CRIME
IS YET UNKNOWN
Further Shortage Has
Been Discovered.
ABSENT HEIRS WERE ROBBED
HINT THAT OTHER OFFICIALS
MAY BE IMPLICATED.
The Known Additional Defalcation
Amounts to More Than $1200.
Some Questionable
Methods.
Treasurer Truman has discovered the
alarming fact that the full extent of ex-
Treasurer 'Widber's defalcation Is as yet
unknown. Enough is known to make it
certain that something more than $1200
must be added to the one hundred and
sixteen thousand odd dollars that Wid
ber admits having stolen from the vaults
of the city treasury and appropriated to
his own use. This additional sum be
longed to absent' heirs whose bequests
and legacies have from time to time been
deposited with the Treasurer by order of
court.
Facts connected with the discovery of
this additional defalcation are such as
to lead the officials to suspect that Wld
ber was not alone in his peculations, and
that some other person in authority un
der him was aware of the manner in
which the city was being robbed and may
even have been a party to the robbery. At
present the officials refuse to discuss this
phase of the case, but hints are thrown
out that future developments may serve
to implicate others than Widber In at
least a part of the defalcation.
The discovery also leads to the conclu
sion that the investigation of the books
and accounts of the Treasurer which was
made after Widber's defalcation became
known was not sufficiently thorough to in
sure the discovery of the entire amount
of the disgraced official's peculations.
How it came about that the "absent
heirs" account was overlooked by these
investigators and police officials is a prob
lem, the solution of which may throw
some additional light on the hints of com
plicity that are now being indulged in.
The present discovery was made
through the demand of the heirs of the
estate of Margaret Sullivan, deceased, for
the sum of $643 TO, deposited with the
Treasurer in 1896 by the administratrix
of the estate. When the demand was
made it was found that there was ncr
money in the treasury to the. credit of
these heirs, although the heirs produced
a receipt signed by Treasurer Widber ac
knowledging the receipt of the money.
A search among the papers found in
Widber's safe after his arrest brought to
light an order of court to the effect that
the money belonging to these particular
heirs be deposited with the Treasurer,
and also a copy of the receipt now held
by the heirs. This copy Is in the hand
writing of Louis Jacobs, who was one of
Widber's deputies. The receipt reads as
follows:
â¢V. â¢'-: SAN FRANCISCO. August 7, 1896.
Received from Julia Conroy, administratrix
of the estate of Margaret Sullivan, â  deceased,
six hundred and forty-three and 70-100 dollars
($643 70), said money being shares of said es
tate of Phillip Harrington and Daniel Harring
ton, whose whereabouts Is unknown.
Share of Daniel Harrington $321 85
Share of I'hillip Harrington .'.. 321 85
Total 1643 70
Phillip and Daniel Harrington now ap
pear on the scene, with their attorney,
J. F. Riley, and demand their patrimony
at the hands of the Treasurer. As there
is no money in the "absent heirs' " fund,
except such as has come in since the ar
rest of Widber, the Messrs. Harrington
will be forced to sue the city for the
money due them.
Strange as it may seem, "Widber kept
no book account of money paid him on
account of absent heirs. The only evi
dence that can be found in the Treas
urer's office to show that he ever re
ceived any moneys on this account are
some papers â orders of court and copies
of receipts similar to those in the case
of the Harrington heirsâ which were
found in the safe in his private office.
An examination of these papers and the
records, in the office of the County Clerk
show that there was paid to him on ac
count of absent heirs various sums as
follows:
Estate of Eliza Choll $295 70
Estate of Edward Browne ISO 86
Estate of Maria Newman 1 00
Estate of Stanhope Dickinson 100 00
Estate of Margaret Sullivan 643 70
Total U. 221 26
Not a cent of this money was found in
the treasury after Widber's arrest, and
the fact that it should have been there
seems to have been overlooked by those
who had charge of the investigation that
followed. Should the heirs to these es
tates come forward and demand the
money due them they would find nothing
to satisfy their claims.
It ie not known whether or not other
moneys were deposited with Widber to
this account, all that is now apparent be
ing the fact that he received the sums
named. A long and tedious search of the
probate records in the Counts Clerk's of
floe, covering the period of Widber's in
cumbency, will be necessary before ' it
will be definitely known just how much
money was paid Into his office by order of
court on account of absent heirs.
Treasurer Truman has made another
discovery that will probably lead to the
disclosure of a still further shortage in i
the ex-Treasurer's accounts. It Is the
matter of escheated estates where small
sums of money are involved. Money
drawn from this source is paid to the
Treasurer and he is required by law to
remit the same to the State Treasurer.
It is asserted that in at least one in
stance, where the sum of $26 is Involved,
Widber's books show that the money was
paid by him to the State Treasurer, but
the books of thÂ« State official prove that
the money was not so paid. This account
and others of similar nature are being in
vestigated, and it will be several days be
fore the full facts are known.
WIDBER ASSUMES
ALL THE BLAME
SAN RAFAEL, April 28.â Widber was
interviewed at San Quentin Prison to
night in reference to the foregoing and
made the following statement: "Mr. de
la Montanya, so I understand, was the
man who went over my accounts with
Mayor Phelan immediately after the
shortage was* discovered, and made a
compilation tixing the actual and total
amount of the shortage. His total was
the aggregate of all the sources from
which money was missing, and he should
be in a position to know all about the
records and whether this $1200 has any
thing to do with the aggregate or not.
"1 presumed this grand total represent
ed all the discoveries of shortages, but
this item may have been overlooked. I
have not been near the office for a year,
and cannot tell whether this is another
itnm or not on account of the fact that
the aggregate was divided into shortages
referring to the various funds. The fart
of any particular clerk signing a receipt
does not necessarily imply that he was
responsible for the shortage. I do not
remember anything about this particular
item, but the responsibility fell upon me
at the time and rests upon me now, and
this new discovery, if it is a new one
would not help me any hy having the re
sponsibility thrown upon anybody else
This much I will say, however: The office
was open the morning after I left, and be
tween the time It was opened and the
time it was closed and sealed by Mayor
Phelan and Auditor Broderick there was
an excellent chance for any of the clerks
to abstract money from the vaults. Al
though this is true, I do not wish it to
be understood that T imply any of the
funds were tampered with during that
time. It is mentioned merely as a possi
bility and not as a fact."
Widber was very anxious to have all

Baseball To-Bay.
The tail-enders. Sacramento and Oak
land, will come together at Recreation
Park this afternoon for the first of a
series of three games that promise to be
frauÃh* with more than passing interest
to the baseball public. Both teams owe
their present unfortunate position to a
series of accidents, bvit they have made
special efforts for this series. Sacramento
has signed some new men, including Har
vey, the great left-hand pitcher, and is
trying to lure Truck Egan from San Jose.
The Oakland cripples, with the exception
of Dtmleavy, are again in good shape and
prepared to fight the baseball battle of
their lives. The teams are evenly
matched, and when in anything like their
normal condition are capable of putting
up as good ball as any team in the league.

The Young Will Contest.
A contest to the will of the late Thomas
Young, who died recently, leaving an es
tate valued at about $40,000, was filed yes
terday by his daughter, Margaret J. Bes
ton. The plaintiff alleges that her father
was of unsound mind when he made the
testament, in which the contestant is dis
inherited, and also that undue influence
was exercised over him by his second
wife. Annie Young, to whom was be
queathed the bulk of the estate.
